## Query

50年代至今，中国大陆中国古代文学研究头部学者知识背景差异调查

## Report

# Scholars 1980S 1990S

# Leading Scholars in Chinese Ancient Literature: 1980s-1990s Reform Generation (改革开放时期头部学者)

## Overview

The 1980s-1990s cohort of Chinese ancient literature scholars represents a pivotal generation that rebuilt and transformed the discipline after the Cultural Revolution (1966-1976). This generation, often called the "Reform and Opening New Wave" (改革开放新锐), emerged during a unique historical moment when the resumption of the National College Entrance Examination (gaokao) in 1977 and the restoration of graduate education in 1978 created new pathways for intellectual development ([Wikipedia - Yuan Xingpei](https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/袁行霈)). These scholars benefited from direct mentorship by the founding generation of 1950s scholars while simultaneously inheriting the responsibility to reconstruct Chinese humanities after a decade of devastation.

What makes this generation historically significant is that they bridged two eras: they were educated under the new socialist system yet deeply influenced by traditional scholarly methods transmitted by their mentors who had trained in the Republican era (1912-1949). Many received their education during periods of political upheaval, with some scholars' training interrupted by political campaigns, only to resume studies in their 30s or even 40s after the reforms ([Baidu Baike - Mo Lifeng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/莫砺锋/7856017)). This combination of traditional training, political experience, and reform-era opportunities shaped a scholarly generation that would dominate Chinese ancient literature studies from the 1980s through the 2010s.

## Historical and Institutional Context

### The Reconstruction Period

The late 1970s and early 1980s marked a critical turning point for Chinese humanities BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution had devastated academic institutions, scattered faculty, destroyed research materials, and interrupted an entire generation's education. This matters BECAUSE when universities reopened after 1977, there was an urgent need to train a new generation of scholars who could revive traditional disciplines. As a result, the period 1978-1985 saw concentrated efforts to restore graduate programs, with universities like Peking University, Fudan University, Nanjing University, and Beijing Normal University becoming centers for rebuilding Chinese ancient literature studies ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)).

The restoration of the graduate degree system in 1978-1981 was transformative BECAUSE China had suspended degree programs entirely since 1966, creating a fifteen-year gap in scholarly training. This matters BECAUSE the first cohort of graduate students admitted after 1978 (often in their 30s or 40s, having spent the Cultural Revolution in factories or countryside) became the core of the 1980s-1990s scholarly generation. As a result, institutions rapidly established master's and doctoral programs, with the first batch of doctoral degrees awarded in 1983-1984 - making these recipients pioneers who would shape the discipline for decades ([Baidu Baike - Mo Lifeng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/莫砺锋/7856017)).

### Institutional Traditions and Regional Schools

Several major institutions developed distinctive scholarly traditions that shaped this generation:

| Institution | Scholarly Tradition | Representative Scholars | Key Characteristics |
|------------|---------------------|------------------------|---------------------|
| Peking University | Comprehensive literary history, aesthetic criticism | Yuan Xingpei, Ge Xiaoyin | Integration of historical and aesthetic approaches ([Wikipedia - Yuan Xingpei](https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/袁行霈)) |
| Nanjing University | Textual scholarship, poetry studies | Mo Lifeng, Cheng Qianfan lineage | Emphasis on philological rigor and close reading ([Baidu Baike - Mo Lifeng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/莫砺锋/7856017)) |
| Fudan University | Literary theory, genre studies | Zhang Peiheng, Wang Yunxi | Theoretical innovation in genre analysis ([Baidu Baike - Zhang Peiheng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/章培恒/2304832)) |
| Zhonghua Book Company | Ancient texts editing, bibliographic studies | Fu Xuancong | Publishing and editorial excellence ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)) |

These regional scholarly traditions mattered BECAUSE they created distinct methodological approaches: Peking University emphasized comprehensive literary history and aesthetic criticism, Nanjing University stressed philological precision and textual scholarship inherited from Cheng Qianfan, and Fudan University pioneered new theoretical frameworks for genre studies. As a result, scholars trained in different institutions brought complementary strengths to the field, enriching Chinese ancient literature studies with methodological diversity.

## Key Scholars: Biographical Data and Academic Lineages

### Yuan Xingpei (袁行霈, b. 1936)

**Institutional Affiliation**: Peking University, Department of Chinese Literature

**Educational Background**: Yuan was born April 18, 1936, in Jinan, Shandong Province, with ancestral roots in Wujin, Jiangsu (modern Changzhou). He entered Peking University in 1953 and graduated from the Chinese Department in 1957 with a bachelor's degree in literature ([Wikipedia - Yuan Xingpei](https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/袁行霈), [Baidu Baike - Yuan Xingpei](https://baike.baidu.com/item/袁行霈/1878173)). This timing is significant BECAUSE Yuan belonged to the first cohort educated entirely under the new PRC system, receiving training during the mid-1950s "golden period" before political campaigns disrupted academia. As a result, he absorbed both traditional literary scholarship and new socialist academic frameworks during his formative years.

**Career Trajectory**: Yuan was retained at Peking University immediately upon graduation in 1957, appointed as an instructor (助教). He was promoted to professor in 1984 and received qualification as doctoral advisor in 1986 ([Baidu Baike - Yuan Xingpei](https://baike.baidu.com/item/袁行霈/1878173)). His career matters BECAUSE he represents continuity: unlike many peers whose careers were interrupted by political campaigns, Yuan maintained his position at Peking University throughout, allowing him to rebuild the department's ancient literature program immediately after 1978.

**Research Specialization**: Yuan specializes in Chinese classical poetry, particularly studies of the aesthetic dimension of ancient Chinese literature. His major works include comprehensive literary histories and studies of major poets across dynasties ([Wikipedia - Yuan Xingpei](https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/袁行霈)).

**Academic Lineage**: Yuan represents direct institutional continuity with Peking University's pre-1949 scholarly tradition, though specific advisor information is not detailed in available sources. His role as chief editor of *History of Chinese Literature* positions him as a discipline leader who shaped how entire generations study literary history.

**Honors and Recognition**: Yuan served as Director of the Peking University Institute for Chinese Studies, became a Boya Distinguished Professor (博雅讲席教授) - Peking University's highest academic honor - and was appointed Director of the Central Research Institute of Culture and History (中央文史研究馆馆长) in 2006 ([Baidu Baike - Yuan Xingpei](https://baike.baidu.com/item/袁行霈/1878173), [Wikipedia - Yuan Xingpei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuan_Xingpei)). He also served in significant political roles including the 8th and 9th National Committee of the Chinese People's Political Consultative Conference (CPPCC) and the 10th National People's Congress Standing Committee, indicating his influence extended beyond academia into cultural policy.

### Mo Lifeng (莫砺锋, b. 1949)

**Institutional Affiliation**: Nanjing University, Department of Chinese Literature

**Educational Background**: Mo Lifeng was born in April 1949 in Wuxi, Jiangsu Province. His educational path exemplifies this generation's struggles and resilience: his formal education was interrupted by the Cultural Revolution. He graduated from Nanjing University's Chinese Ancient Literature program in October 1984 with a doctoral degree, making him one of the first literary doctorates awarded in the People's Republic of China (新中国首批文学博士) ([Baidu Baike - Mo Lifeng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/莫砺锋/7856017), [Wikipedia - Mo Lifeng](https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/莫砺锋)). This matters enormously BECAUSE Mo's doctoral degree represented the restoration of China's graduate education system after a 15-year hiatus, making him a symbolic figure of academic recovery.

**Career Trajectory**: Mo served as Director of the Nanjing University Chinese Department, Director of the China Poetry Research Center (中国诗学研究中心), and founding Dean of the Mei'an Academy (梅庵书院). He was designated as a Senior Professor in the Humanities and Social Sciences (人文社科资深教授) in 2014 - a title granted only to the most distinguished scholars ([Baidu Baike - Mo Lifeng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/莫砺锋/7856017)). In 2022, he retired from his teaching position, and in October 2024, he was appointed Director of the Jiangsu Province Museum of Literature and History (江苏省文史研究馆馆长), continuing his scholarly influence in retirement.

**Research Specialization**: Mo specializes in Song Dynasty poetry, particularly the works of Du Fu and Song Dynasty literary culture. He is also known as a popular lecturer on CCTV's "Lecture Room" (百家讲坛) program, making classical literature accessible to general audiences ([Baidu Baike - Mo Lifeng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/莫砺锋/7856017)).

**Academic Lineage**: Mo Lifeng is most significantly known as a disciple of Cheng Qianfan (程千帆, 1913-2000), one of the most important literary scholars of the 20th century ([Wikipedia - Cheng Qianfan](https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/程千帆)). Cheng Qianfan himself was trained in the Republican era and represented the transmission of traditional philological methods. This lineage matters BECAUSE Cheng's rigorous textual and historical methods were transmitted directly to Mo, who then passed them to subsequent generations, creating a clear scholarly lineage spanning from Republican-era training through reform-era reconstruction. Mo's training under Cheng represents the direct transmission of traditional Chinese scholarly methods (考据学, evidential scholarship) into the post-Mao era.

**Significance**: Mo Lifeng's career trajectory - from Cultural Revolution disruption to becoming China's first literary doctorate to becoming a media figure popularizing classical literature - embodies the transformation of Chinese humanities in the reform era. His work bridges rigorous academic scholarship with public engagement, representing a new model of scholarly influence.

### Zhang Peiheng (章培恒, 1934-2011)

**Institutional Affiliation**: Fudan University, Department of Chinese Literature

**Educational Background**: Zhang Peiheng was born January 8, 1934, in Shaoxing, Zhejiang Province. Details of his undergraduate education are less documented in the available sources, but he emerged as a leading scholar at Fudan University during the 1980s ([Baidu Baike - Zhang Peiheng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/章培恒/2304832)).

**Career Trajectory**: Zhang was awarded the title "Middle-Aged and Young Expert with Outstanding Contributions" by the State Council Personnel Department in 1984 - a prestigious early-career recognition that identified him as a key figure in disciplinary reconstruction. In 1996, he received the titles of both Chief Professor (首席教授) and Distinguished Professor (杰出教授) at Fudan University, the university's highest academic honors ([Baidu Baike - Zhang Peiheng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/章培恒/2304832)). He served as Director of the Center for Ancient Chinese Literature Research (中国古代文学研究中心主任) and Director of the Institute for Ancient Text Collation and Research (古籍整理研究所所长), as well as Director of the Fudan University Chinese Language and Literature Postdoctoral Station. He passed away on June 7, 2011, at age 77 ([Baidu Baike - Zhang Peiheng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/章培恒/2304832)).

**Research Specialization**: Zhang pioneered new approaches to Chinese literary history, particularly in genre theory and the social functions of literature. He is known for theoretical innovations in understanding the development of Chinese fictional and dramatic genres.

**Academic Leadership**: Beyond his research, Zhang held significant administrative roles including Vice-Chair of the Ministry of Education's Humanities and Social Sciences Expert Advisory Committee (教育部人文社会科学专家咨询委员会副主任委员) and Vice-Chair of the Ministry of Education's National Committee for Ancient Text Collation and Research in Higher Education Institutions (教育部全国高等院校古籍整理研究工作委员会副主任委员) ([Baidu Baike - Zhang Peiheng](https://baike.baidu.com/item/章培恒/2304832)). These positions gave him substantial influence over national research priorities and resource allocation in ancient Chinese literature.

**Significance**: Zhang's career illustrates how the 1980s-1990s generation didn't merely restore traditional scholarship but actively innovated, developing new theoretical frameworks for understanding Chinese literary history.

### Fu Xuancong (傅璇琮, 1933-2016)

**Institutional Affiliation**: Zhonghua Book Company (中华书局), later Tsinghua University

**Educational Background**: Fu Xuancong (pen name Zhan Zhi/湛之) was born November 18, 1933, in Ningbo, Zhejiang Province. He entered Tsinghua University's Chinese Department in 1951, but due to institutional restructuring (院系调整) in 1952, he transferred to Peking University's Chinese Department, graduating in 1955 ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)). He remained at Peking University as an assistant instructor after graduation.

**Career Trajectory**: Fu's career took an unexpected turn in 1958 when he was transferred to Zhonghua Book Company during a political campaign. What began as punishment became his life's work: he rose through the editorial ranks to become Editorial Department Director, then Deputy Chief Editor, and finally Chief Editor (总编辑) of Zhonghua Book Company - China's most prestigious publisher of ancient texts ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)). This position gave him extraordinary influence over which ancient texts were published and how they were edited, effectively shaping what materials were available to the entire field. In 2008, he returned to Tsinghua University to establish and direct the Center for Research in Classical Chinese Literature and Documents (古典文献研究中心), serving as professor and doctoral advisor. He passed away on January 23, 2016, at age 83.

**Research Specialization**: Fu specialized in Tang-Song literature and the relationship between the civil service examination system (科举) and literary culture. His major works include *Studies of Tang Dynasty Poets* (《唐代诗人丛考》), *The Tang Dynasty Civil Service Examination and Literature* (《唐代科举与文学》) - which won the Third Si Mian Original Works Prize (第三届思勉原创奖) - and *Chronological Biography of Li Deyu* (《李德裕年谱》) ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)).

**Editorial Leadership**: Fu's most lasting impact may be his editorial work on massive projects including chief editor of *Continuation of the Complete Library of the Four Treasuries* (《续修四库全书》), *Complete Poetry of the Song Dynasty* (《全宋诗》), and *Comprehensive Catalog of Chinese Ancient Texts* (《中国古籍总目》). He also edited scholarly series like *Annotated Edition of Biographies of Tang Talents* (《唐才子传校笺》) and *Annotated Edition of Biographies of Song Talents* (《宋才子传笺证》) ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)).

**Institutional Roles**: Fu served as Secretary-General and later Deputy Director of the State Council's Ancient Books Publication Planning Group (国务院古籍整理出版规划小组秘书长、副组长), giving him authority over national priorities in ancient text publication. He was also a member of the Central Research Institute of Culture and History (中央文史馆馆员) ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)).

**Significance**: Fu represents a unique hybrid career combining rigorous scholarship with editorial and institutional leadership. His work as publisher and project director shaped the infrastructure of the field, determining which texts and research projects received resources and attention. His transition from "punished intellectual" sent to publishing house in 1958 to becoming China's most influential academic publisher illustrates the complex trajectories of this generation.

## Additional Major Scholars (Summary Data)

While detailed biographical information is limited in the sources accessed, the following scholars are recognized as leading figures from this generation:

### Ge Xiaoyin (葛晓音)
- **Institution**: Peking University
- **Specialization**: Six Dynasties and Tang Dynasty poetry
- **Significance**: Represents the continued strength of Peking University's poetry studies tradition

### Wang Yunxi (王运熙) and others at Fudan
- **Institution**: Fudan University
- **Significance**: Part of the Fudan school's theoretical innovations in literary history

### Jiang Yin (蒋寅)
- **Institution**: Chinese Academy of Social Sciences
- **Specialization**: Qing Dynasty poetry and poetics
- **Significance**: Represents the development of research institutes alongside university programs

### Chen Shangjun (陈尚君)
- **Institution**: Fudan University
- **Specialization**: Tang Dynasty literature and bibliographic studies
- **Significance**: Known for rigorous textual work and discovery of lost Tang texts

### Luo Zongqiang (罗宗强)
- **Institution**: Nankai University
- **Specialization**: History of Chinese literary thought
- **Significance**: Pioneered studies of pre-modern literary theory and criticism

### Yu Xianhao (郁贤皓)
- **Institution**: Nanjing Normal University
- **Specialization**: Tang Dynasty literature
- **Significance**: Key figure in Tang literature studies outside the top-tier universities

## Generational Characteristics

### Educational Background Patterns

This generation's educational trajectories reveal several patterns:

1. **Age diversity**: Unlike typical academic cohorts, this generation spans a wide age range (born 1930s-1950s) BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution disrupted normal progression. Some earned degrees in their 40s alongside others in their 20s. This matters BECAUSE it created unusual mentor-student dynamics and brought diverse life experiences into academic work.

2. **Interrupted education**: Many had their studies interrupted by political campaigns, then resumed after 1978. This disruption matters BECAUSE it meant scholars developed resilience and often possessed real-world experiences (factory work, rural labor) that informed their understanding of literature's social functions. As a result, this generation combined scholarly rigor with practical awareness of literature's role in society.

3. **Direct lineage from pre-1949 scholars**: Many were taught by scholars trained in the Republican era or earlier, creating direct transmission of traditional methods. This lineage matters BECAUSE it preserved methodological continuity despite political upheaval. As a result, traditional philological rigor was transmitted into the reform era, preventing complete rupture with earlier scholarly traditions.

4. **Pioneer status**: Several scholars, particularly Mo Lifeng, were among China's first doctoral degree recipients after 1983-1984. This status matters BECAUSE they had to establish standards and expectations for doctoral research when few models existed. As a result, they shaped what doctoral-level work in ancient Chinese literature would mean for subsequent generations.

### Methodological Approaches

This generation's scholarship exhibits several distinctive features:

1. **Integration of traditional and modern methods**: They combined traditional philological approaches (textual criticism, historical research) with modern literary theory BECAUSE they inherited classical methods from their teachers while also being exposed to Western literary theory introduced after 1978. This integration matters BECAUSE it created a distinctively Chinese approach to literary studies that was rigorous in textual scholarship while engaging with theoretical questions. As a result, their work achieves depth in both textual understanding and interpretive sophistication.

2. **Large-scale collaborative projects**: This generation organized massive collaborative projects like comprehensive literary histories, complete collections of dynastic poetry, and encyclopedic reference works ([Baidu Baike - Fu Xuancong](https://baike.baidu.com/item/傅璇琮/1880179)). These projects emerged BECAUSE the field required systematic reconstruction of basic resources destroyed during the Cultural Revolution. This matters BECAUSE these collective efforts created shared infrastructure (databases, reference works, critical editions) that enabled subsequent research. As a result, younger scholars inherited comprehensive resources their predecessors lacked.

3. **Institution-building**: Beyond individual research, this generation rebuilt departments, established research centers, created academic journals, and developed graduate programs BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution had devastated institutional infrastructure. This work matters BECAUSE it created the organizational structures that would support future generations. As a result, by the 1990s, China possessed a robust network of research institutions in ancient literature.

### Political and Institutional Roles

Unlike earlier generations who focused primarily on research, many in this cohort held significant administrative and advisory positions:

- Several served on the Chinese People's Political Consultative Conference (CPPCC) or National People's Congress (NPC)
- Many directed national-level research projects funded by the National Social Science Fund (国家社科基金)
- Some held positions in cultural policy organizations like the Central Research Institute of Culture and History

This political engagement matters BECAUSE it gave scholars direct influence over cultural policy and resource allocation. As a result, academic priorities could be translated into institutional realities through funding decisions, publication priorities, and educational policies.

## Research Output and Major Projects

This generation's major collective achievements include:

### Comprehensive Literary Histories
- Multi-volume comprehensive histories of Chinese literature that became standard references
- Specialized period studies (Tang, Song, Ming-Qing literature)

### Complete Collections and Anthologies
- *Complete Poetry of the Song Dynasty* (《全宋诗》)
- *Continuation of the Complete Library of the Four Treasuries* (《续修四库全书》)
- Various critical editions of classical texts

### Reference Works and Tools
- *Comprehensive Catalog of Chinese Ancient Texts* (《中国古籍总目》)
- Annotated editions of major literary works
- Biographical dictionaries of writers

These projects matter BECAUSE they created the basic infrastructure of the field - the reference works, databases, and critical editions that all subsequent research depends upon. The generation's legacy is thus not only in individual monographs but in the collective resources they created.

## Historical Significance

The 1980s-1990s generation of Chinese ancient literature scholars holds unique historical significance for several reasons:

1. **Disciplinary Reconstruction**: They rebuilt an entire academic field from ruins BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution had devastated humanities education and research. This matters BECAUSE without their systematic reconstruction efforts, subsequent generations would have lacked both institutional support and research resources. As a result, by the late 1990s, Chinese ancient literature studies had not only recovered but expanded beyond pre-1966 levels.

2. **Methodological Bridge**: They synthesized traditional Chinese scholarly methods with modern approaches BECAUSE they were uniquely positioned between generations: trained by Republican-era scholars but working in the reform era with access to international scholarship. This synthesis matters BECAUSE it created a distinctively Chinese approach to literary studies that was both rooted in tradition and engaged with contemporary theory. As a result, Chinese ancient literature studies developed a coherent methodology that differed from both purely traditional and purely Western approaches.

3. **Generational Transmission**: They trained the next generation (1990s-2000s) who would lead the field into the 21st century. This transmission matters BECAUSE they established standards, methods, and institutional structures that would persist. As a result, their influence extends far beyond their own publications into the scholarly culture they created.

4. **International Engagement**: Many in this generation spent time abroad (Harvard-Yenching Institute, universities in Japan, Singapore, Hong Kong) and facilitated international exchange BECAUSE the reform era opened China to academic cooperation. This international engagement matters BECAUSE it reconnected Chinese scholarship with global conversations after decades of isolation. As a result, Chinese ancient literature studies re-entered international sinological discourse.

## Conclusion

The 1980s-1990s generation of leading scholars in Chinese ancient literature represents a historically unique cohort that rebuilt their discipline under exceptional circumstances. Born in the 1930s-1950s, educated despite political upheaval, and coming to prominence during the reform era, they served as a bridge between traditional scholarship and modern academia. Their legacy encompasses not only important individual research but also the institutional structures, research resources, and scholarly standards that continue to shape Chinese ancient literature studies.

Understanding this generation's knowledge backgrounds reveals how Chinese humanities reconstructed themselves after political catastrophe: through direct lineage from pre-1949 scholars, systematic rebuilding of institutions and resources, synthesis of traditional and modern methods, and commitment to training subsequent generations. Their careers demonstrate that scholarly traditions can survive disruption through determined reconstruction efforts and that academic fields are built not only through research but through institution-building, resource development, and generational transmission of methods and values.

A complete survey would require access to specialized academic databases, university archives, and detailed biographical dictionaries that document the full range of leading scholars from this generation.
