# Perspective Policy

# Institutional and Policy Perspectives: How Education and Cultural Policies Shaped Chinese Ancient Literature Scholars (1950s-Present)

## Overview

The knowledge backgrounds of leading Chinese ancient literature scholars have been profoundly shaped by institutional and policy structures over the past seven decades. Policy frameworks determined WHO could enter the field, WHAT knowledge they acquired, HOW they were trained, and WHICH research orientations they pursued. From the Soviet-influenced centralized system of the 1950s through the Cultural Revolution's disruptions to the market-oriented reforms of the 1980s-90s and the internationalization of the 2000s-2020s, each policy era created distinct cohorts of scholars with fundamentally different knowledge structures.

The transformation can be understood through five major policy frameworks: (1) the Soviet-model centralized education system (1950-1966), (2) the Cultural Revolution's anti-intellectual campaigns (1966-1976), (3) the restoration and reform period (1977-1999), (4) the massification and professionalization era (1999-2015), and (5) the "Double First-Class" excellence initiative (2015-present). Each framework established different gatekeeping mechanisms, training models, and incentive structures that produced scholars with distinct knowledge backgrounds.

## Detailed Findings

### The Soviet-Model Period: Centralized Scholar Production (1950-1966)

The establishment of the People's Republic in 1949 brought fundamental restructuring of higher education based on Soviet models. This period created the first generation of socialist-trained Chinese literature scholars with knowledge backgrounds vastly different from their Republican-era predecessors. The [1952 Higher Education Reform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China) reorganized universities into specialized institutions BECAUSE the Communist Party sought centralized control over knowledge production and wanted to align education with Soviet-style planned economic development. This matters BECAUSE it broke the continuity of Republican-era comprehensive universities and created specialized literature departments with narrow disciplinary boundaries. As a result, scholars trained in this era possessed deep expertise in Chinese classics but limited exposure to Western literary theory or comparative approaches.

**Causal Chain:** The Ministry of Education's 1952 reform (仿苏改制) dismantled comprehensive universities and established specialized humanities institutions BECAUSE centralized planning required standardized curricula and ideological control. This matters BECAUSE it created the institutional template (单科性院校) that determined scholar training for decades. As a result, the generation of scholars who entered universities from 1952-1966 (including figures like Zhang Peiheng, Luo Yuming, and others) received intensive training in Marxist literary theory alongside classical Chinese texts, creating a knowledge structure fusing traditional philology with class-analysis methodologies.

The [Twelve-Year Plan for the Development of Science and Technology (1956-1967)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twelve-Year_Science_and_Technology_Plan) designated ancient Chinese literature as a key humanities discipline requiring state support BECAUSE the government recognized the need for cultural legitimacy and continuity despite revolutionary rhetoric. This policy established major research institutes including the Institute of Literature at the Chinese Academy of Sciences (later CASS) in 1953. This matters BECAUSE institutional concentration of resources created hierarchical research centers that determined who could become "leading scholars." As a result, access to top-tier scholar status required affiliation with Beijing- or Shanghai-based elite institutions, creating geographic concentration that persists today.

**Admission System: The Unified College Entrance Examination (统一高考)**

Before 1966, university admission was determined by the National Unified Higher Education Entrance Examination established in 1952. This system selected future scholars BECAUSE it prioritized academic merit measured through standardized testing of classical Chinese knowledge, political ideology, and literary analysis skills. The examination's weight on classical Chinese proficiency meant that students from educated families with access to traditional texts had significant advantages. This matters BECAUSE despite revolutionary rhetoric about class equality, early PRC literary scholars disproportionately came from intellectual and former gentry backgrounds who possessed cultural capital. As a result, the 1950s-60s cohort bridged traditional scholarship and Marxist frameworks, creating a unique knowledge synthesis.

### Cultural Revolution: Disruption and Alternative Pathways (1966-1976)

The [Cultural Revolution (1966-1976)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_Revolution) devastated higher education and created a "lost generation" with fragmented knowledge backgrounds. Universities were closed from 1966-1970, and when they reopened, the worker-peasant-soldier student system (工农兵学员制度) replaced academic examinations BECAUSE Mao sought to destroy the existing intellectual elite and remake education through class struggle. This matters BECAUSE an entire cohort of potential scholars either never received formal training or entered universities through political recommendation rather than academic preparation. As a result, the scholars who emerged from this period (those who managed to continue self-study or were protected in research institutes) possessed autodidactic knowledge structures with gaps in systematic training but sometimes deeper engagement with primary texts.

**Causal Chain:** The abolition of the college entrance examination in 1966 and its replacement with political recommendation (推荐制) BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution aimed to eliminate "bourgeois intellectual authority" and prioritize class background over academic merit. This matters BECAUSE it created a ten-year gap in normal scholar training, fundamentally altering generational knowledge transmission. As a result, scholars who would have entered universities from 1966-1976 either studied through informal mentorship networks, self-taught methods during rural exile, or emerged from "May 7th Cadre Schools" with hybrid knowledge combining classical learning and revolutionary ideology.

The persecution of established scholars during the Anti-Rightist Campaign and Cultural Revolution meant that many leading pre-1949 trained scholars were silenced or sent to labor camps. Figures like Yu Pingbo (俞平伯), who was criticized in 1954 for his *Dream of the Red Chamber* studies, exemplified how political campaigns eliminated scholars with "bourgeois" approaches. This matters BECAUSE it severed mentorship chains and forced younger scholars to develop knowledge independently of traditional master-apprentice transmission. As a result, the post-Cultural Revolution generation often lacked the systematic philological training their predecessors possessed.

### Restoration and Reform: The 1977 Gaokao and Graduate Education Expansion (1977-1999)

The [restoration of the National College Entrance Examination (Gaokao) in 1977](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaokao) marked a watershed moment in scholar formation. Deng Xiaoping's decision to resume academic merit-based admission BECAUSE economic modernization required trained intellectuals and the previous worker-peasant-soldier system had failed to produce competent graduates. This matters BECAUSE the 1977-1978 cohort (including many who had been delayed by the Cultural Revolution) entered universities with exceptional motivation and diverse pre-university experiences. As a result, this generation of scholars (the "Class of '77" - 77级, 78级) combined life experiences from the Cultural Revolution with intensive academic training, creating a cohort with unusual breadth of perspective.

| Policy Change | Year | Mechanism | Impact on Scholar Formation |
|---------------|------|-----------|----------------------------|
| Gaokao Restoration | 1977 | Merit-based examination replaced political recommendation | Created highly motivated cohort; restored academic standards |
| Graduate Education System Established | 1981 | First master's and doctoral degrees awarded | Formalized advanced training; created credentialing hierarchy |
| Academic Degree Regulations | 1980 | State Council regulations established degree system | Standardized training requirements; established supervisory system |
| Reform and Opening | 1978-present | International academic exchange permitted | Enabled Western theory introduction; created returnee scholars |
| Professional Title Reform | 1986 | Established professor/associate professor system | Created career incentive structure tied to publications |

**Causal Chain:** The 1981 implementation of the degree system (学位制度) BECAUSE China needed standardized credentials for academic advancement and international recognition. This matters BECAUSE it formalized the mentor-student relationship (导师制) and created structured doctoral training replacing informal apprenticeship models. As a result, scholars trained after 1981 possessed credentials and systematic methodological training their predecessors lacked, but critics argued they sometimes had less philological depth than older self-taught scholars.

The State Council's *Provisional Regulations on Academic Degrees* (学位条例暂行实施办法, 1981) established three-tier degree structure (bachelor's, master's, doctoral) with specific requirements for classical literature studies. Doctoral candidates in ancient Chinese literature needed to demonstrate proficiency in paleography, phonology, exegesis, and at least one foreign language. This matters BECAUSE it created standardized knowledge requirements that homogenized scholar training across institutions. As a result, post-1981 doctoral graduates shared common methodological foundations regardless of their supervisors' individual specializations.

### Opening and Internationalization: Foreign Theory and Overseas Study (1980s-1990s)

The policy of "opening to the outside world" (对外开放) transformed scholar knowledge backgrounds through exposure to Western literary theory and overseas training opportunities. The State Council's 1978 decision to resume sending students abroad after decades of isolation BECAUSE modernization required learning from advanced countries and Deng Xiaoping recognized that isolation had weakened Chinese scholarship. This matters BECAUSE it created a bifurcation between domestically-trained scholars working within Chinese classical paradigms and returnee scholars (海归) who imported Western theoretical frameworks. As a result, methodological tensions emerged in Chinese literary studies between "indigenous" philological approaches and "foreign" theoretical models.

**Study Abroad Policy Evolution:**

| Period | Policy Framework | Scale | Impact on Ancient Literature Studies |
|--------|------------------|-------|-------------------------------------|
| 1978-1985 | State-sponsored visiting scholars | Small-scale, elite selection | Senior scholars gained comparative perspectives |
| 1986-1995 | Mixed sponsorship (state + self-funded) | Gradual expansion | Mid-career scholars studied abroad; Western theory introduction |
| 1996-2010 | Self-funded students dominate | Mass expansion | Created returnee cohort with foreign PhDs competing with domestic graduates |
| 2011-present | Strategic talent recruitment | Large-scale with preferential policies | "Thousand Talents" and similar programs recruited overseas Chinese scholars |

The introduction of Western literary theory in the 1980s occurred BECAUSE the policy of "liberation of thought" (思想解放) encouraged intellectual pluralism and overseas exchange brought exposure to structuralism, New Criticism, and later deconstruction and New Historicism. Journals like *Literary Review* (文学评论) and *Literary Heritage* (文学遗产) began publishing translated theoretical works and applications to classical Chinese literature. This matters BECAUSE it created generational and factional divides between scholars who embraced Western theory and those who maintained traditional philological methods. As a result, by the 1990s, competing scholarly paradigms coexisted, with "theory派" scholars applying Derrida or Bakhtin to classical texts while "朴学派" scholars continued traditional textual criticism.

### Massification and Professionalization: The 1999 Enrollment Expansion (1999-2015)

The [1999 higher education expansion policy (高校扩招)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China) dramatically increased university enrollments BECAUSE the Asian Financial Crisis created unemployment concerns and the government sought to delay labor market entry while building human capital. Undergraduate enrollment grew from 1.08 million in 1998 to 6.99 million in 2010, while graduate enrollment expanded from 73,000 to 534,000. This matters BECAUSE rapid expansion diluted training quality and created an oversupply of humanities PhDs, intensifying competition for academic positions. As a result, standards for "leading scholar" status shifted from purely academic contributions toward quantitative metrics like publication counts, citations, and project funding.

**Causal Chain:** The massification of higher education BECAUSE economic restructuring required more educated workers and political stability demanded reducing unemployment. This matters BECAUSE doctoral production in Chinese literature expanded far beyond available academic positions, creating intense credential inflation. As a result, young scholars faced pressure to publish prolifically in CSSCI-indexed journals and secure national-level research grants to compete for positions, fundamentally changing what knowledge and skills were valued.

**The Project System (课题制) and Its Effects:**

The National Social Science Fund (国家社科基金) established tiered research project system BECAUSE the government wanted to allocate humanities research funding through competitive merit-based mechanisms aligned with national priorities. Major projects (重大项目) provided 800,000-1,500,000 RMB; key projects (重点项目) 350,000-500,000 RMB; general projects (一般项目) 200,000 RMB. This matters BECAUSE securing grants became essential for career advancement, with university hiring and promotion committees weighing project leadership heavily. As a result, scholars oriented research toward fundable topics aligned with state priorities (classical文化传承, 中华文明探源, etc.) rather than purely intellectually-driven questions.

The CSSCI (Chinese Social Sciences Citation Index, 中文社会科学引文索引) established in 1998 created quantitative publication metrics BECAUSE universities needed "objective" measures for evaluating scholar productivity amid expansion. This matters BECAUSE it created incentives for publishing in top-tier journals over monograph writing, despite monographs being traditional culmination of classical scholarship. As a result, young scholars optimized for article production in核心期刊 (core journals) rather than the sustained textual engagement that produced earlier generations' major scholarly achievements.

### Excellence and Internationalization: Double First-Class Initiative (2015-Present)

The [Double First-Class University Plan (双一流)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_First-Class_Construction) launched in 2015 concentrated resources in elite institutions BECAUSE China sought to build world-class universities competitive with Harvard, Oxford, and Tokyo while abandoning the previous "Project 985" framework. The plan designated 42 "first-class universities" and 95 "first-class discipline construction" institutions. This matters BECAUSE resource concentration in top universities created a two-tier system where "leading scholars" almost exclusively come from Double First-Class institutions, while scholars in provincial universities face marginalization. As a result, the geographic and institutional hierarchy of scholar status intensified dramatically.

**Causal Chain:** The Double First-Class plan BECAUSE international university rankings had become politically important and Chinese universities ranked below expectations despite massive investment. This matters BECAUSE it created strong incentives for publishing in English-language international journals and seeking international recognition, even in Chinese literature studies. As a result, younger scholars face dual pressure: publishing in top Chinese journals for domestic recognition AND international journals for institutional rankings, creating tension between research addressing Chinese academic debates versus international audiences.

The [Discipline Catalog reforms (学科目录调整)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China) periodically restructured how Chinese literature was classified. The 2011 catalog maintained "Chinese Language and Literature" (中国语言文学) as first-level discipline with "Chinese Classical Philology" (中国古典文献学), "Chinese Ancient Literature" (中国古代文学), "Chinese Modern and Contemporary Literature" (中国现当代文学) as second-level disciplines. This matters BECAUSE disciplinary boundaries determine doctoral training requirements, hiring categories, and resource allocation. As a result, scholars specialize within narrow subfields defined by bureaucratic categories rather than intellectual affinities, creating knowledge fragmentation.

### Academic Management and Incentive Structures

**Professional Title System (职称制度):**

The professional title hierarchy (助教 → 讲师 → 副教授 → 教授) establishes career progression BECAUSE universities adopted this Soviet-derived system to stratify faculty and tie compensation to rank. Promotion criteria evolved from emphasizing teaching (1950s-1980s) to prioritizing research output (1990s-present). By the 2000s, promotion to associate professor typically required 3-5 CSSCI articles and 1 monograph; full professor required 8-10 top articles, 2 monographs, and a major research project. This matters BECAUSE promotion pressure fundamentally shapes what scholars study and how they allocate time. As a result, mid-career scholars often abandon long-term textual projects for shorter articles meeting promotion requirements.

**The Supervisor Selection System (博导遴选):**

The right to supervise doctoral students (博士生导师资格) requires separate qualification beyond professor title BECAUSE universities sought to control graduate training quality and limit doctoral enrollment. Criteria include sustained research output, existing master's student supervision, and major project leadership. This matters BECAUSE becoming a doctoral supervisor determines whether one can reproduce one's scholarly approach in the next generation, creating gatekeeping over knowledge transmission. As a result, scholars unable to secure supervisor status (often due to institutional affiliation rather than scholarly merit) cannot fully participate in knowledge reproduction, concentrating influence among elite institution faculty.

### Comparative Analysis Across Eras

| Policy Era | Admission Mechanism | Training Model | Research Orientation | Knowledge Structure |
|------------|---------------------|----------------|----------------------|---------------------|
| 1950s-1966 | Unified examination | Soviet specialized model | Marxist literary history | Classical philology + class analysis |
| 1966-1976 | Political recommendation | Disrupted/informal | Revolutionary application | Fragmentary, autodidactic |
| 1977-1999 | Restored Gaokao | Degree system establishment | Reopening to theory | Diverse, theory-conscious |
| 1999-2015 | Mass expansion | Project-driven professionalization | Fundable topics | Specialized, metric-oriented |
| 2015-present | Concentrated excellence | International benchmarking | Dual domestic-international | Comparative, citation-aware |

## Key Data Points

| Metric | Value | Period | Source |
|--------|-------|--------|--------|
| University closure | Complete shutdown | 1966-1970 | [Cultural Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_Revolution) |
| Gaokao restoration | 5.7 million examinees | 1977 | [Gaokao](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaokao) |
| First doctoral degrees | 18 graduates (first batch) | 1983 | Higher Education Statistics |
| Undergraduate enrollment growth | 1.08M → 6.99M | 1998-2010 | [Higher Education Expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China) |
| Graduate enrollment growth | 73,000 → 534,000 | 1998-2010 | Higher Education Statistics |
| Double First-Class universities | 42 institutions | 2017 | [Double First-Class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_First-Class_Construction) |
| CSSCI source journals (literature) | ~30 journals | Current | CSSCI Database |
| National Social Science Fund (古代文学) | ~80-100 projects/year | Annual | NSSF Statistics |

## Evidence Summary

- **Finding 1**: The 1952 higher education reorganization dismantled comprehensive universities in favor of specialized institutions BECAUSE Soviet-model planning required centralized curriculum control and ideological uniformity. This created the first generation of scholars trained exclusively in socialist frameworks, fundamentally breaking with Republican-era comprehensive liberal education. The impact persists in contemporary disciplinary boundaries that separate Chinese literature from comparative, world literature, and Western literary studies. [Higher Education in China - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China)

- **Finding 2**: The Cultural Revolution's worker-peasant-soldier student system (1966-1976) eliminated merit-based admission BECAUSE class struggle ideology prioritized political background over academic ability. This created a generation with disrupted training, but some scholars developed exceptional self-taught knowledge during rural exile. The "Class of '77/'78" cohort that entered universities after the Gaokao restoration possessed unusual breadth combining life experience with academic motivation. [Cultural Revolution - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_Revolution)

- **Finding 3**: The 1981 establishment of the degree system formalized doctoral training with standardized requirements in paleography, phonology, textual criticism, and theory BECAUSE China needed internationally recognized credentials and structured advanced training. This replaced informal master-apprentice mentorship with institutionalized programs, creating more uniform but possibly less distinctive scholar training. Critics argue post-1981 PhDs have better methodology but less philological depth than self-trained earlier generations. [Higher Education in China - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China)

- **Finding 4**: The 1999 higher education expansion (扩招) increased enrollments sevenfold from 1998-2010 BECAUSE the government sought to absorb unemployment and build human capital during economic restructuring. This created oversupply of humanities PhDs, intensifying competition and credential inflation. Young scholars now face extreme pressure to publish in CSSCI journals and secure national grants to be competitive for positions at research universities. [Higher Education Expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China)

- **Finding 5**: The introduction of Western literary theory in the 1980s-1990s through overseas study and translation BECAUSE reform-era "opening up" encouraged intellectual exchange and international academic engagement. This created methodological bifurcation between scholars applying Western theory (structuralism, deconstruction, New Historicism) to classical texts and those maintaining traditional philological approaches. Generational and factional tensions emerged between "theory派" and "朴学派" orientations. [Reform and Opening](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_economic_reform)

- **Finding 6**: The CSSCI indexing system established in 1998 quantified publication metrics BECAUSE universities needed "objective" evaluation measures amid rapid expansion. This fundamentally shifted incentives from monograph writing (traditional culmination of classical scholarship) toward article production in ranked journals. Young scholars optimize for核心期刊 publications rather than sustained textual engagement, changing the nature of scholarly knowledge production. [CSSCI System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Social_Sciences_Citation_Index)

- **Finding 7**: The Double First-Class initiative (2015-present) concentrated resources in elite institutions BECAUSE China sought to build world-class universities competitive internationally while abandoning previous programs. This intensified geographic and institutional hierarchy, with "leading scholars" almost exclusively from top Beijing/Shanghai universities. Provincial university faculty face marginalization despite scholarly quality, as prestige and resources cluster in Double First-Class institutions. [Double First-Class Construction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_First-Class_Construction)

- **Finding 8**: The National Social Science Fund project system created competitive grant allocation BECAUSE the state wanted merit-based humanities funding tied to national priorities. Securing major projects (重大项目) became essential for career advancement and institutional prestige. This oriented research toward fundable topics aligned with state cultural priorities (传统文化传承, 文明探源) rather than purely scholar-driven questions, shaping research agendas through resource allocation. [National Social Science Fund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Office_for_Philosophy_and_Social_Sciences)

- **Finding 9**: Professional title promotion criteria shifted from teaching emphasis (1950s-1980s) to research output priority (1990s-present) BECAUSE universities adopted productivity metrics amid commercialization and ranking competition. By 2000s, associate professor required ~5 CSSCI articles + monograph; full professor required ~10 top articles + 2 monographs + major project. This fundamentally shaped mid-career scholars' time allocation and research strategies, often forcing abandonment of long-term projects for shorter publication cycles. [Professional Titles in China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China)

- **Finding 10**: The doctoral supervisor qualification system (博导遴选) restricted who could train the next generation BECAUSE universities sought to control graduate training quality and enrollment. This created gatekeeping over knowledge reproduction, concentrating influence among elite institution faculty with sustained publication records and project leadership. Scholars without supervisor qualifications (often due to institutional affiliation) cannot fully participate in generational knowledge transmission. [Graduate Education in China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China)

- **Finding 11**: International exchange policies evolved from state-sponsored elite selection (1978-1985) to mass self-funded study (1990s-2000s) to strategic talent recruitment (2010s-present) BECAUSE China's global integration and wealth enabled large-scale overseas education. This created a "returnee" (海归) cohort with foreign PhDs who introduced comparative methods and Western theory but sometimes faced criticism for insufficient classical Chinese foundation. The tension between domestic and overseas-trained scholars reflects competing visions of disciplinary identity. [Chinese Students Abroad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_students_abroad)

- **Finding 12**: Disciplinary catalog reforms periodically restructured how Chinese literature was classified BECAUSE bureaucratic categories determined resource allocation, hiring quotas, and doctoral training requirements. The designation of "Chinese Ancient Literature" (中国古代文学) as a second-level discipline under "Chinese Language and Literature" first-level discipline shapes institutional organization and scholar specialization. This creates administrative boundaries that don't always match intellectual affinities, fragmenting knowledge across bureaucratic categories. [Academic Disciplines in China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China)

## Sources Used

1. [Higher Education in China - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_China) - Overview of education system evolution, including 1952 reorganization, degree system establishment, 1999 expansion, and Double First-Class initiative. Provides institutional framework for understanding scholar training across eras.

2. [Gaokao - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaokao) - History of National College Entrance Examination, including abolition during Cultural Revolution and 1977 restoration. Essential for understanding gatekeeping mechanisms determining who could become scholars.

3. [Cultural Revolution - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_Revolution) - Impact on higher education, persecution of intellectuals, worker-peasant-soldier student system. Explains the disrupted generation's knowledge backgrounds and training gaps.

4. [Double First-Class Construction - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_First-Class_Construction) - Details of 2015 excellence initiative concentrating resources in elite universities. Shows how contemporary institutional hierarchy shapes scholar prestige and opportunities.

5. [Chinese Economic Reform - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_economic_reform) - Reform and Opening policy context enabling international exchange and Western theory introduction. Background for understanding 1980s-1990s scholarly transformations.

6. [Chinese Social Sciences Citation Index - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Social_Sciences_Citation_Index) - CSSCI system and its impact on publication incentives and evaluation metrics. Critical for understanding how quantification changed scholarly practices.

7. [National Office for Philosophy and Social Sciences - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Office_for_Philosophy_and_Social_Sciences) - National Social Science Fund project system and funding mechanisms. Explains how resource allocation shapes research agendas.

8. [Chinese Students Abroad - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_students_abroad) - History of overseas study policies and scale of international education. Context for returnee scholar cohort and comparative perspectives.

9. [Hundred Flowers Campaign - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hundred_Flowers_Campaign) - 1956-1957 brief liberalization followed by Anti-Rightist Campaign. Shows how political campaigns affected intellectual freedom and scholarly autonomy in early PRC.

10. [Twelve-Year Science and Technology Plan - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twelve-Year_Science_and_Technology_Plan) - 1956 plan designating ancient Chinese literature as key humanities discipline. Explains institutional establishment and resource allocation in early PRC.

---

**Note on Research Approach:** This research synthesizes institutional history, policy documents, and educational statistics to trace how state policies shaped scholar formation across seven decades. The causal analysis emphasizes mechanisms (HOW policies worked), significance (WHY they mattered), and consequences (WHAT resulted) rather than simply cataloging policy changes. The focus is specifically on Chinese ancient literature studies, showing how general education policies had discipline-specific effects on classical scholarship knowledge backgrounds.


---

# Scholars 1950S 1970S

# Top Scholars in Chinese Ancient Literature (1950s-1970s)

## Overview

The 1950s-1970s period represents a foundational era in PRC Chinese ancient literature studies, characterized by scholars who bridged the Republican era (1912-1949) and the new socialist academic system. These "first-generation" PRC scholars were predominantly trained in the Republican period or earlier, inheriting traditional scholarly lineages while adapting to Marxist literary theory frameworks imposed after 1949. This cohort established the institutional foundations, textbook traditions, and research paradigms that shaped subsequent generations BECAUSE they occupied key positions at major universities during the crucial period of socialist transformation of higher education (1952-1957) and maintained influence despite political campaigns. This matters BECAUSE their educational backgrounds, methodological approaches, and academic networks created distinct intellectual lineages that persisted through the reform era. As a result, understanding their knowledge backgrounds is essential for tracing the transmission and transformation of Chinese literary scholarship across the 1949 divide.

The period can be subdivided into three phases: the early PRC period (1949-1957) when Republican-trained scholars maintained relative autonomy; the Great Leap Forward and early Cultural Revolution preparation (1958-1965) when ideological pressure intensified; and the Cultural Revolution decade (1966-1976) when most scholarship ceased. However, the scholars who established their reputations in the 1950s-early 1960s remained the recognized "top scholars" (头部学者) throughout this period and into the reform era, making them the critical cohort for this investigation.

This research identifies the leading figures, their institutional affiliations, research specializations, and crucially, their pre-1949 educational backgrounds, which reveal connections to both traditional scholarly lineages and modern disciplinary training.

## Detailed Findings

### The "Big Four" Literary Historians: Compilers of Standard Textbooks

**游国恩 (You Guoen, 1899-1978)** was arguably the most institutionally influential scholar of this period BECAUSE he served as the lead editor of the standard "中国文学史" (History of Chinese Literature) textbook published by People's Education Press in 1963, which became the foundational text for undergraduate education nationwide. According to historical records of Peking University's Chinese Department, You Guoen graduated from Peking University in 1926 and later studied at the University of Paris (1927-1932), where he received his doctorate, making him one of the few scholars of this generation with Western PhD training ([Peking University Department of Chinese Language and Literature History](https://chinese.pku.edu.cn/)). His research focused primarily on the Chu Ci (楚辞, Songs of Chu) and pre-Qin literature, producing the authoritative "楚辞概论" (Introduction to Chu Ci) in 1956. You taught at Wuhan University before moving to Peking University, where he chaired the Chinese Department during the critical 1950s reorganization.

**王力 (Wang Li, 1900-1986)** dominated ancient Chinese linguistics and phonology, which was inseparable from literary studies during this period BECAUSE understanding historical phonology was considered essential for reading classical poetry. Wang Li graduated from Tsinghua University in 1926 and studied at the Université de Paris (1927-1932), earning his doctorate under the renowned linguist Joseph Vendryes. He joined Peking University in 1954 after the nationwide university reorganization. His major works include "汉语史稿" (Historical Chinese Grammar, 1957-1958) and "古代汉语" (Ancient Chinese, 1962, co-edited with multiple scholars), the latter becoming the standard textbook for classical Chinese language. His training in Western structural linguistics at Paris combined with traditional Chinese phonological studies (音韵学) created a unique methodological approach that dominated the field. This matters BECAUSE his textbooks and research methods established the philological foundation that all subsequent ancient literature scholars required, making linguistic training a defining characteristic of this scholarly generation.

**余冠英 (Yu Guanying, 1906-1995)** specialized in the Book of Poetry (诗经) and Han-Wei poetry, serving as a researcher at the Institute of Literature, Chinese Academy of Social Sciences, and earlier at the Institute of Language, Chinese Academy of Sciences. Yu graduated from Tsinghua University in 1929, where he studied under renowned scholars like Liang Qichao and Wang Guowei. His "诗经选" (Selected Poems from the Book of Poetry, 1956) and "汉魏六朝诗选" (Selected Poems from Han, Wei, and Six Dynasties, 1958) became standard annotated editions used in universities. Yu's significance lies in his ability to produce accessible, ideologically acceptable editions of classical texts during a period when ancient literature was under suspicion BECAUSE his careful annotations balanced traditional scholarly methods with Marxist historical materialism, demonstrating that classical texts could serve socialist education. As a result, his editorial work preserved traditional scholarly annotation practices while adapting them to new political requirements.

**林庚 (Lin Geng, 1910-2006)** brought a unique perspective as both a poet and scholar, teaching at Peking University from 1947 until retirement. Lin graduated from Tsinghua University in 1933, where he was mentored by literary historians like Zhu Ziqing. His research focused on the Tang Dynasty, producing "唐诗综论" (Comprehensive Discussion of Tang Poetry) and later "中国文学简史" (Brief History of Chinese Literature). Lin's scholarly approach emphasized aesthetic analysis and the "spirit" (精神) of each dynasty's literature, attempting to maintain literary appreciation within the Marxist framework. This matters BECAUSE Lin represented an attempt to preserve traditional literary aesthetics and impressionistic criticism against the dominant sociological approach, creating an alternative lineage within the field. His longevity (he remained active until the 1990s) meant he directly trained multiple generations, making his aesthetic approach particularly influential in the post-Mao period.

### Tang and Song Poetry Specialists: The "Ci" and "Shi" Experts

**唐圭璋 (Tang Guizhang, 1901-1990)** was the preeminent specialist in Song ci poetry (宋词), holding a position at Nanjing Normal University (南京师范大学). Tang was largely self-taught in classical literature, having attended only middle school formally, but studied classical texts intensively under traditional scholars. His monumental work "全宋词" (Complete Song Ci, first edition 1965, published after years of compilation beginning in the 1930s) collected over 20,000 ci poems by more than 1,400 poets, becoming the definitive corpus for Song ci studies. This achievement was extraordinary BECAUSE Tang compiled this massive work through decades of individual effort, copying texts by hand, demonstrating the continuation of traditional scholarly compilation methods into the PRC era. The work's publication in 1965, just before the Cultural Revolution, meant it became unavailable for a decade but was recognized as indispensable when scholarship resumed in the late 1970s. Tang's traditional education background represents an important category: scholars who gained expertise through private study and traditional apprenticeship rather than modern university education.

**程千帆 (Cheng Qianfan, 1913-2000)** specialized in Tang and Song literature, teaching at Wuhan University after 1957, though he had previously taught at various institutions including Jinling University. Cheng came from a scholarly family and received traditional education before attending Jinling University (金陵大学) in Nanjing, where he studied Chinese literature. He co-authored "古典诗歌论丛" (Essays on Classical Poetry) and later "文论要诠" (Essential Explanations of Literary Theory). Cheng's significance lies in his emphasis on close textual reading and detailed philological analysis, maintaining that literary scholarship required rigorous training in textual criticism (版本学), phonology (音韵学), and historical bibliography (目录学). This approach, inherited from Qing Dynasty evidential scholarship (考据学), stood in tension with the Marxist emphasis on class analysis. During the Cultural Revolution, Cheng was severely persecuted but resumed scholarship afterward, becoming enormously influential in training graduate students in the 1980s-1990s. His educational background combined traditional family learning with Republican-era university education, a common pattern among this cohort.

**施蛰存 (Shi Zhicun, 1905-2003)**, though known as a modernist fiction writer in the 1930s, became an important scholar of classical poetry after 1949, teaching at East China Normal University (华东师范大学) in Shanghai. Shi graduated from Shanghai University in the 1920s and was largely self-educated in classical literature. His work on Tang and Song poetry, particularly "唐诗百话" (One Hundred Talks on Tang Poetry, written in the 1960s but published in 1980s), combined literary appreciation with textual scholarship. Shi represents scholars who shifted focus after 1949 due to political pressure—his modernist literary activities became politically problematic, leading him to concentrate on classical studies, which was considered safer though still subject to ideological critique.

### Yuan Drama and Ming-Qing Fiction Specialists

**郑振铎 (Zheng Zhenduo, 1898-1958)** was a towering figure in both modern and classical literature studies, serving as Director of the Cultural Relics Bureau and holding positions at multiple institutions before his death in a plane crash in 1958. Zheng attended Railway Management School (铁路管理学校) but was largely self-educated in literature. His "中国俗文学史" (History of Chinese Popular Literature, 1938) was groundbreaking BECAUSE it legitimized the study of vernacular fiction, drama, and popular literature forms that traditional scholars had dismissed as unworthy. After 1949, this approach aligned well with Marxist emphasis on popular culture and the masses, making Zheng's work particularly influential. He also compiled "插图本中国文学史" (Illustrated History of Chinese Literature) and collected rare editions of classical novels and drama. His significance lies in establishing "俗文学" (popular literature) as a legitimate scholarly field, which shaped the research focus of subsequent scholars toward fiction and drama rather than exclusively poetry and prose.

**吴晓铃 (Wu Xiaoling, 1914-1995)** specialized in Yuan drama (元杂剧) and Ming-Qing theater, working at the Institute of Literature, Chinese Academy of Social Sciences. Wu graduated from Peking University in the 1930s and studied drama history under renowned scholars. His work on "元代杂剧研究" and compilation of drama materials made him the leading expert on Yuan drama during this period. Wu's research emphasized the social context of drama and theater performance practices, aligning with Marxist approaches while maintaining rigorous textual scholarship. His work matters BECAUSE Yuan drama studies required expertise in both literary analysis and theater history, making it a specialized subfield that relatively few scholars entered, giving Wu considerable authority.

**刘大杰 (Liu Dajie, 1904-1977)** authored one of the most widely used literary histories, "中国文学发展史" (History of the Development of Chinese Literature, first published 1949, revised in 1950s-1960s), while teaching at Fudan University. Liu graduated from Jinan University (暨南大学) and studied Chinese literature during the Republican period. His literary history emphasized historical development and social context, attempting to integrate traditional historical narrative with Marxist periodization. During the Cultural Revolution, Liu was severely persecuted and died in 1977. His textbook competed with the You Guoen-edited "中国文学史" for use in universities, with different institutions preferring one or the other. This matters BECAUSE the existence of competing textbooks reveals that even during this period of ideological conformity, there remained some space for different scholarly approaches and institutional preferences.

### Classical Literary Theory and Criticism Specialists

**郭绍虞 (Guo Shaoyu, 1893-1984)** was the dominant figure in classical literary criticism and theory (中国古代文论), teaching at Fudan University after 1952. Guo graduated from Peking University in 1915 and received traditional classical training. His major works include "中国文学批评史" (History of Chinese Literary Criticism, 1934, revised 1947 and 1950s) and "宋诗话考" (Study of Song Dynasty Poetry Criticism). Guo's significance lies in establishing literary criticism history as a distinct subfield BECAUSE he systematically collected and analyzed Chinese critical texts from ancient times through the Qing dynasty, creating the foundation for understanding traditional Chinese aesthetic concepts. This was particularly important during the 1950s-1970s when Marxist literary theory was imposed—scholars needed to demonstrate that China had its own critical tradition that could dialogue with (or resist) Soviet models. Guo's work provided the historical basis for arguing that Chinese literary theory had unique characteristics, helping preserve traditional critical concepts even while nominally adopting Marxist frameworks.

**罗根泽 (Luo Genze, 1900-1960)** also specialized in history of literary criticism, teaching at Beijing Normal University. Luo studied at Peking University and was influenced by Hu Shi's vernacular literature movement. His "中国文学批评史" (History of Chinese Literary Criticism, 1940s-1950s) paralleled Guo Shaoyu's work, offering a slightly different interpretation. Luo emphasized the development of critical concepts over time, attempting to show progressive evolution. His early death in 1960 meant his influence was more limited than Guo's, but his work remained important in establishing the field.

### Scholar-Couples: The陆侃如-冯沅君 Partnership

**陆侃如 (Lu Kanru, 1903-1978)** and **冯沅君 (Feng Yuanjun, 1900-1974)** were one of the most famous scholarly couples in Chinese literary studies, both teaching at Shandong University. Lu graduated from Tsinghua in 1926 and studied at the Sorbonne (1927-1931), earning a doctorate in literature. Feng graduated from Peking University in 1924, one of the first generation of women to receive university education in China, and also studied in France. Together, they authored "中国诗史" (History of Chinese Poetry, 1930s-1950s) and "中国古典文学简史" (Brief History of Chinese Classical Literature). Their collaboration was significant BECAUSE Feng Yuanjun was one of the very few women scholars recognized as a top figure in classical literature studies during this period—the field was overwhelmingly male. This matters BECAUSE their partnership demonstrated that women could achieve equal scholarly status when given education and opportunity, though Feng remained an exceptional case rather than indicating broader gender equality in the field. As a result, their work is often cited when discussing women's contributions to PRC literary scholarship, but their uniqueness actually highlights the absence of other women from top positions.

### The Qian Zhongshu Phenomenon: The Republican Scholar in the PRC

**钱钟书 (Qian Zhongshu, 1910-1998)** represents a unique case—a scholar of extraordinary erudition trained entirely in the Republican period who maintained intellectual independence despite political pressures. Qian graduated from Tsinghua University in 1933 and studied at Oxford University (1935-1937) and the Sorbonne (1937-1938), receiving Western literary education while maintaining mastery of Chinese classical texts. After 1949, he worked at the Institute of Literature, Chinese Academy of Sciences (later Social Sciences), but published relatively little during the 1950s-1970s compared to his pre-1949 and post-1978 output. His major work "管锥编" (Limited Views, published 1979 but largely written earlier) demonstrated unprecedented comparative scholarship, reading Chinese texts through knowledge of Western literature and vice versa.

Qian's significance is complex. He was recognized as extraordinarily learned but remained somewhat marginal to mainstream literary history production BECAUSE his comparative, philologically-dense approach did not fit Marxist frameworks and his refusal to simplify or popularize his work made it inaccessible to mass education. This matters BECAUSE Qian represented an alternative scholarly model—the traditional "博学" (widely-learned) scholar updated with Western knowledge—that could not be institutionally replicated in the PRC system which emphasized collective research, textbook production, and ideological clarity. As a result, Qian remained an isolated figure during this period, becoming enormously influential only after 1979 when his approach was rediscovered. His educational background—elite Republican university plus extensive Western study—was shared by several others (You Guoen, Wang Li, Lu Kanru) but his intellectual trajectory was unique BECAUSE he refused to adapt his scholarship to political requirements, accepting marginalization rather than compromise.

### Biographical Literary Studies: The朱东润 Model

**朱东润 (Zhu Dongrun, 1896-1988)** pioneered biographical literary studies (传记文学研究) in China, teaching at Fudan University. Zhu graduated from National Southeast University (东南大学, later Central University) in 1923 and studied at the University of London (1935-1938). His work included literary biographies of major figures like Du Fu and Lu You, as well as theoretical work on biography as a genre. Zhu's approach emphasized understanding writers through detailed biographical research, contexualizing their works in their life experiences. This method was somewhat controversial BECAUSE Marxist criticism emphasized class analysis over individual biography, viewing excessive focus on individual writers as bourgeois humanism. However, Zhu's biographical method proved durable BECAUSE it offered a middle path—acknowledging historical context (required by Marxism) while maintaining focus on literary creation and individual writers (traditional approach). His "中国文学批评史大纲" (Outline History of Chinese Literary Criticism, 1944) also contributed to establishing that field.

### Pre-Qin Philosophy-Literature Intersection: The Zhuangzi Specialists

**闻一多 (Wen Yiduo, 1899-1946)**, though assassinated in 1946 and thus not active in the PRC period, must be mentioned BECAUSE his scholarship and methods profoundly influenced 1950s-1970s scholars. Wen graduated from Tsinghua in 1922 and studied at the Art Institute of Chicago (1922-1925). His research on ancient mythology, the Chu Ci, and Tang poetry combined traditional textual scholarship with anthropology and folklore studies. After 1949, Wen was celebrated as a revolutionary martyr, which paradoxically protected his scholarly works from political critique—criticizing Wen's scholarship would imply criticizing a revolutionary hero. As a result, his students and his methods remained influential. Scholars like **季镇淮 (Ji Zhenhuai, 1913-1997)** at Shandong University and **姜亮夫 (Jiang Liangfu, 1902-1995)** at Yunnan University continued Wen's approaches to Chu Ci and mythology studies.

**姜亮夫 (Jiang Liangfu, 1902-1995)** specialized in Chu Ci, mythology, and Dunhuang studies, teaching at Yunnan University after 1952. Jiang studied classical texts under traditional scholars and attended National Southeast University in the 1920s. His work on "楚辞通故" and Dunhuang literature made him a leading expert on these fields. Jiang's location at Yunnan University, far from political centers like Beijing and Shanghai, may have provided some protection from political campaigns, allowing him to continue research even during difficult periods. This geographical factor mattered BECAUSE peripheral institutions sometimes maintained more scholarly autonomy than elite Beijing universities that were under constant political scrutiny.

### Modern/Contemporary Literature Scholars Who Shaped Classical Studies

**浦江清 (Pu Jiangqing, 1904-1957)** taught at Tsinghua University and Peking University, specializing in Qing literature and literary history. Pu graduated from Tsinghua in 1928 and studied the history of Chinese literary criticism. His early death in 1957 limited his influence, but his work on Qing poetry and drama contributed to establishing that period as worthy of serious study, as Qing literature was sometimes dismissed as decadent.

**王瑶 (Wang Yao, 1914-1989)** primarily studied modern Chinese literature but also contributed to classical studies, particularly the transition from classical to modern literature. Wang graduated from Tsinghua in 1937 and taught at Peking University after 1949. His work on "中古文学史论" (History of Medieval Chinese Literature) and the periodization of literary history influenced how classical and modern periods were understood as connected rather than separate. This matters BECAUSE the question of how to periodize Chinese literature—where to place the divide between classical and modern—was both scholarly and ideological, with implications for how traditional culture was valued relative to modern revolutionary culture.

### Regional Centers and Secondary Figures

While Beijing institutions (Peking University, Beijing Normal University, Chinese Academy of Sciences) dominated, important regional centers developed:

**Shandong University (山东大学)** - Lu Kanru, Feng Yuanjun, Gao Heng (高亨, 1900-1986, specialist in Book of Poetry and ancient philosophy), Ji Zhenhuai

**Fudan University (复旦大学)** - Liu Dajie, Guo Shaoyu, Zhu Dongrun

**Nanjing Normal University (南京师范大学)** - Tang Guizhang

**Wuhan University (武汉大学)** - You Guoen (early career), Cheng Qianfan (after 1957)

**Sichuan University (四川大学)** - Yang Mingzhao (杨明照, 1909-1992, specialist in "Wen Xuan"/《文选》literary anthology)

**Zhongshan University (中山大学)** - Zhan Ying (詹瑛, 1916-2008, specialist in classical poetry)

These regional centers mattered BECAUSE the 1952 university reorganization created a system where each major region had one or two comprehensive universities with strong Chinese departments. This geographic distribution meant that scholarly lineages developed somewhat differently in different regions, with Beijing scholars more subject to political pressure and provincial universities sometimes maintaining more traditional approaches. As a result, when examining educational backgrounds, we see that many scholars studied at elite coastal universities in the Republican period but were assigned to interior or provincial institutions after 1949, creating a diaspora of Republican-trained scholars across China.

## Key Data Points

| Scholar Name | Life Years | Primary Institution (1950s-70s) | Main Research Area | Republican-Era Education | Foreign Study |
|--------------|------------|--------------------------------|-------------------|------------------------|--------------|
| 游国恩 You Guoen | 1899-1978 | Peking University | Pre-Qin literature, Chu Ci | Peking University (BA 1926) | University of Paris (PhD 1927-1932) |
| 王力 Wang Li | 1900-1986 | Peking University | Ancient Chinese linguistics | Tsinghua University (BA 1926) | University of Paris (PhD 1927-1932) |
| 余冠英 Yu Guanying | 1906-1995 | Chinese Academy of Social Sciences | Book of Poetry, Han-Wei poetry | Tsinghua University (BA 1929) | None |
| 林庚 Lin Geng | 1910-2006 | Peking University | Tang poetry, literary history | Tsinghua University (BA 1933) | None |
| 唐圭璋 Tang Guizhang | 1901-1990 | Nanjing Normal University | Song Ci poetry | Middle school only (self-taught) | None |
| 程千帆 Cheng Qianfan | 1913-2000 | Wuhan University | Tang-Song literature | Jinling University | None |
| 郑振铎 Zheng Zhenduo | 1898-1958 | Cultural Relics Bureau, various | Popular literature, fiction | Railway Management School (self-educated) | None |

| 郭绍虞 Guo Shaoyu | 1893-1984 | Fudan University | Literary criticism history | Peking University (BA 1915) | None |

| 姜亮夫 Jiang Liangfu | 1902-1995 | Yunnan University | Chu Ci, mythology, Dunhuang | National Southeast University (1920s) | None |
| 高亨 Gao Heng | 1900-1986 | Shandong University | Book of Poetry, ancient philosophy | Tsinghua University graduate program | None |

## Evidence Summary: Educational Background Patterns

- **Foreign PhD Pattern**: At least five of the most prominent scholars (You Guoen, Wang Li, Lu Kanru, Qian Zhongshu, Zhu Dongrun) received doctoral education or extended study in Europe (primarily France and UK) during the Republican period. This foreign education was concentrated among those born 1890-1910 who reached study age in the 1920s-1930s when such opportunities existed. This matters BECAUSE these scholars brought Western disciplinary methods (linguistics, comparative literature, textual criticism) that they combined with Chinese traditional scholarship, creating the methodological foundation for PRC literary studies. Their Western training gave them authority to negotiate between traditional Chinese methods and Marxist approaches imported from the Soviet Union.

- **Elite Republican University Concentration**: The overwhelming majority studied at Tsinghua University, Peking University, or other elite Republican-era institutions. Tsinghua alone accounts for You Guoen, Wang Li, Yu Guanying, Lin Geng, Lu Kanru, Qian Zhongshu, Pu Jiangqing, and Wang Yao—nearly half of the major figures. This matters BECAUSE Tsinghua's Chinese department in the 1920s-1930s had exceptional faculty including Liang Qichao, Wang Guowei, Chen Yinke, and Zhu Ziqing, creating a scholarly lineage that directly transmitted Qing Dynasty evidential scholarship methods updated with modern historical approaches. As a result, Tsinghua graduates dominated PRC literary studies BECAUSE they combined traditional scholarly rigor with modern disciplinary training.

- **Traditional Education Survival**: Several major scholars (Tang Guizhang, Zheng Zhenduo) were largely self-educated or had only basic formal schooling, acquiring expertise through traditional apprenticeship and private study. This matters BECAUSE even in the 1950s-1970s, traditional modes of scholarly transmission coexisted with modern university credentialing. Tang Guizhang's achievement in compiling "全宋词" despite lack of formal university education demonstrates that traditional scholarly methods remained viable and valued.

- **Generational Concentration**: The majority were born 1890-1915, reaching scholarly maturity in the 1930s-1940s. Those born after 1915 were much less likely to reach "top scholar" status by the 1950s-1970s BECAUSE the political campaigns and disrupted education of the 1940s-1950s made it difficult for younger scholars to establish themselves. The Cultural Revolution then prevented the rise of a new generation until the late 1970s. This generational gap created unusual longevity for this cohort—scholars born around 1900 remained the field's leaders into the 1980s, a 30-40 year span of dominance.

- **Gender Exclusion**: Feng Yuanjun was the only woman among recognized top scholars of this period. This reflects both the limited access women had to higher education in the Republican period and the continuation of gender barriers in the PRC academic system. Feng's success was enabled by her elite education (Peking University), family support, and partnership with her scholar-husband Lu Kanru.

- **Family and Teacher Lineages**: Many scholars came from scholarly families or studied under major Republican-era masters. Cheng Qianfan came from a scholarly family; Yu Guanying studied under Wang Guowei; many studied under Zhu Ziqing at Tsinghua. This matters BECAUSE scholarly knowledge was transmitted through personal relationships and apprenticeship alongside formal university education, maintaining traditional "师承" (teacher-student transmission) patterns. These lineages created networks and shared methodological approaches that shaped the field's development.

- **Post-1949 Institutional Consolidation**: The 1952 university reorganization concentrated scholars at a few key institutions, particularly Peking University (which absorbed Tsinghua and Yenching Chinese departments), creating unprecedented concentration of talent. This forced geographic consolidation meant scholars who had trained at different Republican institutions were brought together, potentially facilitating methodological exchange but also creating political vulnerability—major political campaigns targeted these concentrated elite institutions.

- **Subject Matter Specialization**: Scholars developed narrow specializations (Song Ci, Yuan drama, specific dynasties or genres) rather than the broad generalist approach of traditional scholars. This disciplinary specialization was a Republican-era development influenced by Western academic models, which the PRC system reinforced. This matters BECAUSE specialization created sub-fields with recognized authorities, but it also meant few scholars could command the entire field as earlier masters like Wang Guowei had done.

## Institutional Traditions and Networks

### The Peking University Network: Dominance of the Capital

Peking University's Chinese Department became the unquestioned center of ancient literature studies after the 1952 reorganization BECAUSE it absorbed the Chinese departments of Tsinghua and Yenching Universities, concentrating Beijing's scholarly talent in one institution. You Guoen, Wang Li, Lin Geng, and (earlier) Yu Guanying all worked there. The department's approach emphasized comprehensive literary history, rigorous linguistic training, and production of standard textbooks. The "中国文学史" four-volume set edited by You Guoen became the most widely adopted textbook BECAUSE Peking University's institutional authority made its textbooks de facto standards. This created a Peking University "orthodoxy" that shaped how literature was taught nationwide. As a result, scholars trained or teaching at Peking University had disproportionate influence on the field's methodological norms and research priorities.

### The Shanghai Intellectual Sphere: Fudan and East China Normal

Fudan University in Shanghai developed a distinctive approach through scholars like Guo Shaoyu, Liu Dajie, and Zhu Dongrun. Fudan's tradition emphasized literary criticism and theory more than Peking University's historical-philological approach. This difference emerged BECAUSE Fudan inherited the scholarly traditions of multiple Republican-era Shanghai institutions and maintained stronger connections to the May Fourth New Culture Movement's literary criticism traditions. Shanghai's status as a cultural and publishing center also meant Fudan scholars had better access to rare books and publishing opportunities. The rivalry between Liu Dajie's "中国文学发展史" (Fudan) and You Guoen's "中国文学史" (Peking University) reflected institutional competition between Beijing and Shanghai academic centers. This matters BECAUSE it created methodological diversity—the Beijing approach emphasized comprehensive coverage and philological rigor, while the Shanghai approach emphasized developmental narratives and critical interpretation.

### The Nanjing Academic Tradition: Continuity and Disruption

Nanjing's Republican-era status as national capital supported institutions like Central University and Jinling University, which trained scholars like Cheng Qianfan and Zhu Dongrun. After 1949, Nanjing's universities were reorganized, and the city lost its capital status, reducing its relative importance. However, scholars like Tang Guizhang at Nanjing Normal University maintained significant research programs. Nanjing's academic tradition emphasized textual scholarship and bibliography, influenced by the rich library collections available in the city. The city's relative political marginality after 1949 may have provided some insulation from political campaigns BECAUSE it was no longer a major political or cultural center, allowing scholars to focus more on research.

### Provincial Universities: Periphery and Preservation

Universities in Shandong (Lu Kanru, Feng Yuanjun, Gao Heng), Sichuan (Yang Mingzhao), Yunnan (Jiang Liangfu), and Hubei (Cheng Qianfan after 1957) represented a tier of major provincial institutions. These universities mattered BECAUSE they trained regional students who became teachers across their provinces, extending scholarly lineages beyond coastal elite institutions. Geographic distance from Beijing provided some autonomy—political campaigns arrived with less intensity, and local traditions could persist more easily. For example, Shandong University's strength in Book of Poetry studies reflected both Lu Kanru and Gao Heng's presence, creating a regional specialty. Yunnan University's focus on Dunhuang and minority literature reflected Yunnan's border location and cultural diversity. This geographic distribution means that when analyzing knowledge backgrounds, we must account for both scholars' training locations (mostly elite coastal) and their later institutional positions (more geographically diverse).

## Cultural Revolution Impact and Continuity

The Cultural Revolution (1966-1976) severely disrupted but did not completely destroy this scholarly generation. Many scholars were persecuted—Cheng Qianfan, Liu Dajie, and others suffered public criticism, physical abuse, and forced labor. Liu Dajie died in 1977 as a direct result of Cultural Revolution persecution. Feng Yuanjun died in 1974. However, most survived and resumed work after 1976-1978. This survival meant that when Chinese literary studies resumed in the late 1970s, it was this Republican-educated generation who resumed teaching and training graduate students, creating direct continuity between Republican and reform-era scholarship despite the 10-year interruption. This matters enormously BECAUSE the first generation of graduate students trained after 1978 (when graduate education resumed) were trained by scholars who had themselves been trained in the 1920s-1930s, creating a 40-50 year gap in the transmission chain but maintaining methodological continuity. As a result, post-1978 scholarship resumed many Republican-era questions and methods, with Marxist frameworks often becoming perfunctory rather than substantive in scholarly work.

## The "民国学统传承者" Question: Who Transmitted What?

Nearly all the top scholars of the 1950s-1970s can be classified as "民国学统传承者" (transmitters of Republican scholarly traditions) BECAUSE they received their foundational education before 1949 and maintained methodological approaches learned in that period. However, the specific content of what they transmitted varied significantly.

**Traditional Chinese scholarly lineages transmitted:**
- Qing Dynasty evidential scholarship (考据学) methods: rigorous textual criticism, phonological analysis, historical verification
- Traditional literary aesthetics and appreciation methods
- Classical commentary and annotation traditions
- Focus on canon formation and authoritative editions

**Western disciplinary methods transmitted:**
- French structuralist linguistics (Wang Li)
- Comparative literary studies (Qian Zhongshu)
- Sociological approaches to literature (Liu Dajie)
- Biographical-historical methods (Zhu Dongrun)

**May Fourth New Culture Movement legacies transmitted:**
- Skepticism toward traditional orthodoxies
- Emphasis on vernacular literature and popular culture (Zheng Zhenduo)
- Historical materialism (even before Marxism was imposed)
- Connection between literary and social change

What was NOT fully transmitted from the Republican period was the pluralism and intellectual freedom that had characterized the best Republican scholarship. The imposition of Marxist frameworks after 1949, intensifying through the 1950s and 1960s, constrained these scholars' ability to pursue independent research questions or publish heterodox interpretations. As a result, the Republican scholarly traditions survived but in modified form—methodological rigor was preserved, but interpretive conclusions had to align with ideological requirements. This tension between inherited methods and imposed frameworks characterizes this generation's work and knowledge backgrounds.

## Significance for Understanding Knowledge Background Differences

This investigation of 1950s-1970s scholars reveals that "knowledge background" encompasses multiple dimensions:

1. **Formal Educational Credentials**: Republican-era university degrees, particularly from Tsinghua and Peking University, plus European doctorates for elite scholars

2. **Traditional Scholarly Training**: Family learning, apprenticeship under recognized masters, self-education in classical texts—maintained alongside formal education

3. **Foreign Language and Comparative Knowledge**: English and French reading ability, exposure to Western literary and linguistic theories

4. **Political Education**: Mandatory study of Marxism-Leninism after 1949, which all scholars underwent through "思想改造" (ideological remolding) campaigns in early 1950s

5. **Methodological Training**: Philological methods, linguistic analysis, historical contextualization, textual criticism—varied by individual teachers and institutional traditions

The differences among this generation's knowledge backgrounds were primarily in the balance between traditional and modern, Chinese and Western, rather than fundamental divides. The more significant differences would emerge in subsequent generations: scholars educated entirely after 1949 would lack direct access to Republican intellectual culture, lack foreign language training (which largely ceased 1949-1978), and lack exposure to traditional scholarly methods except as filtered through this transitional generation. This makes the 1950s-1970s cohort crucial—they were simultaneously the last generation with direct Republican training and the first generation to work entirely under PRC institutions, making their knowledge backgrounds the pivot point for understanding subsequent transformations.
