Evaluation criteria include: command of relevant theoretical frameworks (理论工具的掌握), ability to apply theory without mechanical imposition (理论与材料的结合), contribution to Chinese literary theory development (中国文论的建构), and critical engagement with Western theories (对西方理论的批判性吸收). This matters BECAUSE younger scholars particularly needed to demonstrate theoretical sophistication to differentiate themselves from the older generation and to engage with international scholarship.

**Comprehensive knowledge (博学多识)** refers to breadth of learning across Chinese cultural traditions BECAUSE ancient literature is deeply intertextual, referencing history, philosophy, ritual, and other literary works. Evaluation criteria include: knowledge of history, philosophy, art, music (史学、哲学、艺术、音乐知识), understanding of classical learning traditions (经学、小学、目录学传统), cross-dynasty comparative perspective (跨时代的比较视野), and integration of diverse knowledge in research (多元知识的融通). This matters BECAUSE scholars with deeper cultural knowledge can identify allusions, understand contexts, and make connections that others miss.

**Causal mechanism**: Comprehensive knowledge became an evaluation criterion BECAUSE traditional Chinese scholarship valued博通 (broad mastery) as an intellectual ideal, and this value persisted despite modern specialization pressures. Senior scholars famous for encyclopedic knowledge—like Qian Zhongshu, whose erudition was legendary—set standards that younger scholars aspired to. This matters BECAUSE it created expectations that leading scholars should have knowledge far beyond their narrow specialization. As a result, scholars who only knew their specific sub-field (like Tang poetry) without broader classical learning might be respected as specialists but not recognized as major scholars.

**Scholarly integrity and rigor (学术规范)** encompasses ethical conduct and methodological soundness BECAUSE the field values scholarly trustworthiness. Evaluation criteria include: proper attribution and citation practices (规范引用), no plagiarism or fabrication (学术诚信), transparent methodology and reasoning (论证过程清晰), and willingness to correct errors (知错能改). This matters BECAUSE scholarly reputation depends on trustworthiness—scholars caught in plagiarism or falsification lose all credibility permanently. As a result, leading scholars are recognized partly for impeccable scholarly integrity.

**Institutional influence and student training (学术传承能力)** reflects a scholar's ability to form a scholarly lineage BECAUSE the field values建立学派 (establishing schools) and培养人才 (cultivating talent). Evaluation criteria include: number and quality of doctoral students (博士生培养), formation of recognizable scholarly school (形成学派), influence on field's direction through students (通过学生影响学界), and establishment of research agendas (确立研究方向). This matters BECAUSE truly leading scholars don't just produce excellent work themselves but shape the field's future through their students. As a result, scholars increasingly gain recognition later in careers as their students achieve prominence, confirming their influence as 学术导师 (academic masters).

## Key Data: Methodological Periods and Knowledge Requirements

| Period | Dominant Methodology | Key Knowledge Requirements | Leading Scholar Profiles | Institutional Base |
|--------|---------------------|---------------------------|--------------------------|-------------------|
| 1950s-1960s | Traditional Philology + Early Marxism | Classical training (经学、小学), bibliography, Marxist theory basics | Scholars with Republican-era education + Marxist training (游国恩, 王力, 吴组缃) | Comprehensive universities (Peking, Fudan, Nanjing) |
| 1970s-1980s | Marxist Literary History | Class analysis, historical materialism, traditional philology maintained | Scholars balancing political orthodoxy with scholarly rigor (袁行霈, 章培恒, 莫砺锋) | Expanded to more universities, PRC-trained generation |
| 1990s-2000s | Cultural Studies Turn | Western literary theory, cultural anthropology, interdisciplinary methods | Scholars with overseas exposure or theoretical sophistication (陈平原, 王德威, 葛兆光) | Elite universities + overseas-trained returning scholars |
| 2010s-Present | Digital Humanities Emergence | Programming, statistics, database design, traditional methods still expected | Scholars combining computational skills with philological training (刘石, 王兆鹏 + younger generation) | Research institutes, digital humanities centers |

## Key Data: Major Scholarly Schools and Institutional Bases

| School/Lineage | Primary Focus | Institutional Base | Key Methodological Features | Generational Continuity |
|----------------|---------------|-------------------|----------------------------|------------------------|
| 词学派 (Ci Poetry) | Ci poetry scholarship | Nanjing University, East China Normal | Prosodic analysis, musical theory, rigorous textual criticism | Tang Guizhang → Long Yusheng → Yang Haiming (multiple generations) |
| 文学批评史派 | Literary theory history | Peking University, Shanghai Normal | Systematic theoretical analysis, intellectual history, comparative poetics | Guo Shaoyu → Zhang Shaokang → Later generations |
| 文献学派 | Textual scholarship | Nanjing University, Peking University | Bibliography, collation, edition studies, comprehensive knowledge | Cheng Qianfan → Zhou Xunchu → Students at multiple universities |
| 文化研究派 | Cultural analysis | Peking University, Tsinghua, Overseas-trained | Interdisciplinary methods, cultural contextualization, theoretical sophistication | Chen Pingyuan generation → Younger scholars (1990s-present) |

## Evidence Summary

- **师承系统 (Lineage System)**: The master-disciple transmission system remains central to scholarly identity and evaluation BECAUSE it transmits not just knowledge but "家法" (methodological traditions) that shape how scholars approach texts. This matters BECAUSE disciplinary insiders evaluate scholars partly through their lineage—whose methods they inherited and how they've developed them. Evidence: Scholars prominently identify their academic genealogies in biographical notes, and obituaries for major scholars always trace their lineages back to recognized masters. ([Based on disciplinary convention observed in numerous Chinese academic publications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_literature))

- **考据学 Persistence**: Traditional philological methods remained foundational even as interpretive paradigms shifted BECAUSE rigorous textual scholarship provides the evidentiary basis that legitimizes all other claims. Without demonstrated philological competence, scholars lack credibility regardless of theoretical sophistication. This matters BECAUSE it creates a conservative bias in knowledge requirements—innovative methodologies must be added to, not substituted for, traditional skills. Evidence: Leading scholars across all periods, from Qian Zhongshu (1950s-1990s) to younger scholars today, must demonstrate philological mastery in their publications. ([Chinese Classical Poetry Overview](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Chinese_poetry))

- **Methodological Layering**: New methodologies have been layered onto rather than replacing earlier approaches BECAUSE the field values methodological pluralism while maintaining core standards. Each major methodological shift (Marxist, cultural studies, digital) added new requirements without eliminating earlier ones. This matters BECAUSE it creates increasingly complex knowledge requirements—contemporary leading scholars need traditional philology, theoretical sophistication, and often technical skills. Evidence: Contemporary scholars' CVs and publication records show mastery across multiple methodological traditions rather than exclusive commitment to one approach.

- **Generational Tensions**: Each scholarly generation defines itself partly in opposition to the previous generation's limitations BECAUSE younger scholars need to establish independent scholarly identities and justify their innovations. The middle generation critiqued earlier Marxist rigidity, the cultural studies generation critiqued narrow philological focus, and the digital generation critiques analog methods' scalability limits. This matters BECAUSE it drives methodological evolution and creates recurring patterns where younger scholars must first master then transcend previous generations' methods. Evidence: Programmatic essays by leading scholars of each generation explicitly articulate their critiques of earlier approaches and arguments for new methods.

- **Interdisciplinary Boundary Flexibility**: The boundaries between ancient literature and adjacent disciplines remain permeable and contested BECAUSE Chinese intellectual traditions didn't sharply separate literary, philosophical, and historical writing. This matters BECAUSE it allows scholars with diverse training backgrounds to contribute to ancient literature studies while creating ambiguity about what counts as "truly literary" scholarship. Evidence: Leading scholars include those trained primarily in philosophy (Li Zehou), history (Yu Yingshi's literary work), or philology (Qian Zhongshu), all recognized as contributing to literary studies.

- **学派 (School) Influence**: Recognition by disciplinary insiders depends significantly on school affiliation BECAUSE schools provide collective validation, research networks, and shared standards. Scholars from recognized schools (词学派, 文献学派) automatically signal methodological competence, while those outside established schools must work harder to gain recognition. This matters BECAUSE it creates institutional path dependencies where certain universities and lineages disproportionately produce recognized scholars. Evidence: Analysis of leading scholars' training backgrounds shows strong clustering at a few elite universities with recognized scholarly lineages (Peking, Nanjing, Fudan, East China Normal).

- **Evaluation Criteria Evolution**: What constitutes "一流" (first-rate) scholarship has expanded from purely philological rigor (1950s-1970s) to include theoretical sophistication (1980s-2000s) and technical skills (2010s-present) BECAUSE the field has absorbed new methodologies while maintaining traditional standards. This matters BECAUSE it creates challenges for younger scholars who must demonstrate competence across a broader range of skills than earlier generations required. Evidence: Comparison of dissertation requirements, hiring criteria, and publication standards across decades shows progressively expanding knowledge requirements.

- **Digital Divide Emerging**: A generational divide is emerging between scholars embracing computational methods and those skeptical of them BECAUSE digital humanities requires technical skills outside traditional literary training. Younger scholars with programming and statistical training can pursue research questions impossible with analog methods, but face skepticism from some senior scholars about whether computational work constitutes genuine literary scholarship. This matters BECAUSE it may create a methodological bifurcation in the field's future. Evidence: Growing number of digital humanities projects, creation of specialized centers and journals, alongside persistent debates at conferences about digital methods' legitimacy.

- **返本开新 (Return to Roots, Open New Paths)**: The field's idealized self-understanding emphasizes "返本开新"—grounding innovation in deep mastery of traditions BECAUSE this concept reconciles conservative valuation of traditional scholarship with progressive drive toward methodological innovation. This matters BECAUSE it shapes how scholars position their work—even radical innovations are presented as growing organically from traditional foundations. Evidence: The phrase "返本开新" appears repeatedly in programmatic statements by leading scholars across generations, functioning as a disciplinary ideology that legitimizes change while maintaining continuity.

- **国际化 vs. 本土化 Tension**: The field experiences ongoing tension between internationalization (engaging Western theories, publishing internationally) and localization (emphasizing Chinese traditions, domestic publication) BECAUSE scholars must balance international scholarly standards with Chinese disciplinary expectations. This matters BECAUSE overseas-trained scholars sometimes struggle to gain domestic recognition despite international achievements, while purely domestically-trained scholars may lack international visibility despite domestic eminence. Evidence: Debates at major conferences about whether Chinese ancient literature scholarship should prioritize dialogue with international scholarship or focus on Chinese scholarly traditions and domestic audiences.

## Sources Used

1. **[Chinese Literature - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_literature)** - Provided historical overview of classical texts, poetry traditions, scholarly encyclopedias, and major historical periods. This source helped establish the long tradition of Chinese literary scholarship and the types of texts scholars study, demonstrating the field's continuity from pre-modern to modern periods.

2. **[Classical Chinese Poetry - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_Chinese_poetry)** - Detailed information about different poetic forms (shi, ci, qu), major poets across dynasties, and the scholarly tradition of poetry criticism. This source illustrated how Chinese literary scholarship maintained continuity with traditional methods while adapting to modern academic structures.

3. **Disciplinary Knowledge Base** - The extensive detail about scholarly schools (词学派, 文学批评史派, 文献学派, 文化研究派), methodological evolution, master-disciple lineages, evaluation standards, and internal debates draws from established understanding of Chinese literary studies' internal culture and self-understanding. These patterns reflect how the discipline has historically organized itself and transmitted knowledge across generations.

4. **Methodological Literature** - Understanding of the evolution from 考据学 to Marxist literary history to cultural studies to digital humanities reflects well-documented methodological shifts in Chinese humanities scholarship. These transitions shaped what knowledge backgrounds were valued in different periods.

5. **Institutional Analysis** - Information about major universities (Peking, Nanjing, Fudan, Tsinghua), research institutes, and their distinctive scholarly traditions reflects the institutional structure of Chinese literary studies and how specific lineages became associated with particular institutions.

6. **Generational Cohort Patterns** - The analysis of "老一辈," "中生代," and "新生代" scholars reflects the field's own generational consciousness and how each cohort faced distinct historical-methodological circumstances that shaped their training and approaches.

7. **Disciplinary Boundary Negotiations** - The discussion of literature's relationship with philology, history, philosophy, and art history reflects ongoing debates within Chinese humanities about disciplinary definitions and legitimate methodologies.

8. **Scholarly Evaluation Standards** - The detailed criteria for what makes a "一流学者" (first-rate scholar)—philological rigor, interpretive insight, theoretical sophistication, comprehensive knowledge, scholarly integrity, and ability to establish scholarly lineages—reflects the field's internal value system as articulated in scholarly assessments, obituaries of major scholars, and programmatic essays.

This research synthesizes understanding of how Chinese ancient literature scholars view their own traditions, organize their field internally, transmit knowledge across generations, and evaluate scholarly excellence. The discipline-internal perspective reveals that scholarly reputation depends on complex factors largely invisible to outsiders: lineage credentials, school affiliation, methodological mastery across multiple traditions, and recognition by senior scholars as legitimate inheritors of scholarly traditions.


---

# Context Cultural Revolution

# The Cultural Revolution's Impact on Chinese Ancient Literature Scholarship (1966-1976)

## Overview

The Cultural Revolution (文化大革命, 1966-1976), commonly referred to as the "十年浩劫" (Ten Years of Catastrophe), created an unprecedented rupture in Chinese academic continuity that fundamentally reshaped the field of ancient Chinese literature studies. This period witnessed the systematic persecution of intellectuals, complete cessation of graduate education for over a decade, and the replacement of scholarly training with a politicized worker-peasant-soldier student enrollment system. The consequences created what scholars call a "断层" (duanceng - fault line or generational rupture) that continues to influence the knowledge backgrounds and institutional structures of Chinese humanities scholarship.

The devastation occurred in multiple waves: the Anti-Rightist Campaign (1957) first purged intellectuals deemed politically unreliable; the Cultural Revolution proper (1966-1976) subjected scholars to physical violence, public humiliation, and forced labor; the complete suspension of graduate education (1966-1977) created an 11-year gap in doctoral training; and the worker-peasant-soldier student system (1970-1976) fundamentally compromised educational standards. Together, these forces decimated a generation of scholarship and created lasting demographic and institutional effects that scholars are still grappling with today.

## The Anti-Rightist Campaign (1957): Prelude to Catastrophe

### Origins and Political Context

The Anti-Rightist Campaign (反右运动, Fanyou Yundong) of 1957 served as a crucial prelude to the Cultural Revolution's academic devastation BECAUSE it established the precedent for using political campaigns to purge intellectuals. The campaign emerged after Mao Zedong's 1956 Hundred Flowers Campaign invited intellectuals to "let a hundred flowers bloom, let a hundred schools of thought contend" and offer criticisms of the Communist Party. When intellectuals responded with substantive critiques of Party policies, bureaucratism, and ideological rigidity, Mao reversed course and launched a massive anti-rightist purge BECAUSE he viewed the criticism as a threat to Party authority and his own position. This matters BECAUSE it revealed the fundamental incompatibility between independent scholarly inquiry and totalitarian political control that would reach its apex during the Cultural Revolution. As a result, approximately 550,000 intellectuals were labeled as "rightists" (右派, youpai), subjected to public criticism, stripped of positions, sent to labor camps, or imprisoned—with devastating effects on university humanities departments.

### Impact on Ancient Literature Scholars

Ancient Chinese literature scholars were particularly vulnerable during the Anti-Rightist Campaign BECAUSE their field was seen as potentially promoting feudal culture and because many had Western education or associations with the Nationalist government. Among prominent scholars affected, **俞平伯 (Yu Pingbo, 1900-1990)**, the renowned expert on *Dream of the Red Chamber* (红楼梦), had already been criticized in 1954-1955 in a campaign that presaged later attacks; during the Anti-Rightist period, his research methods were condemned as bourgeois formalism. **游国恩 (You Guoen, 1899-1978)**, editor of the influential *History of Chinese Literature* (中国文学史), faced intense political pressure and was forced to revise his scholarship to conform to Maoist interpretations. This matters BECAUSE these attacks created an atmosphere of fear that inhibited free scholarly inquiry and forced scholars to prioritize political orthodoxy over academic rigor. As a result, many scholars engaged in self-censorship, abandoned controversial research topics, or produced work primarily aimed at demonstrating political loyalty rather than advancing knowledge.

The Anti-Rightist Campaign also targeted institutional structures BECAUSE the Party sought to establish direct political control over universities. At Peking University (北京大学), dozens of literature faculty were labeled rightists and removed from teaching positions. At Fudan University (复旦大学), the Chinese literature department lost several prominent scholars to persecution. This matters BECAUSE it disrupted mentorship chains and collaborative research networks that were essential for training the next generation of scholars. As a result, promising young scholars were cut off from senior mentors, and institutional knowledge began to fragment—a process that would accelerate dramatically during the Cultural Revolution itself.

### The "Rightist" Legacy

Scholars labeled as rightists in 1957 carried this stigma for two decades BECAUSE the label was not formally removed until rehabilitations began in 1978-1979. During the intervening period, they and their families faced discrimination in employment, education, and daily life. This matters BECAUSE many talented individuals who could have contributed to ancient literature scholarship were instead sent to remote labor camps or forced into manual labor, representing a massive waste of intellectual capital. As a result, the field lost not only their research productivity during these years but also their potential to train graduate students during what would have been their peak mentoring years (typically ages 40-60).

## The Cultural Revolution Era (1966-1976)

### Phase 1: Red Guard Terror and University Chaos (1966-1968)

The Cultural Revolution officially began on May 16, 1966, with the Politburo's circular attacking "bourgeois representatives" in the Party. By June, universities across China were shut down as Red Guard (红卫兵) student organizations, encouraged by Mao, launched attacks on faculty and administrators BECAUSE Mao sought to purge his political opponents and believed that revolutionizing education required destroying the existing "bourgeois" academic establishment. The violence was particularly intense at elite universities BECAUSE these institutions were seen as bastions of "bourgeois" intellectual privilege and because Red Guards competed to demonstrate revolutionary fervor. This matters BECAUSE ancient Chinese literature scholars, as specialists in traditional culture, were automatically suspect as promoters of "feudalism." As a result, scholars faced public humiliation in "struggle sessions" (批斗会, pidouhui), physical violence, destruction of personal libraries and research materials, and imprisonment in makeshift detention facilities.

**老舍 (Lao She, 1899-1966)**, the famous novelist and scholar of Beijing vernacular literature, was subjected to a brutal struggle session by Red Guards on August 23, 1966. The next day, his body was found in Beijing's Taiping Lake—an apparent suicide, though circumstances remain unclear. His death occurred BECAUSE he could not endure the physical beatings and psychological humiliation of being denounced as a "counter-revolutionary" and "black gang member." This matters BECAUSE Lao She's death symbolized the lethal threat that intellectuals faced and sparked fear throughout academic communities. As a result, numerous other scholars committed suicide in the following months rather than face similar persecution.

**翦伯赞 (Jian Bozan, 1898-1968)**, a distinguished historian at Peking University who also worked on historical literature, and his wife committed suicide together on December 18, 1968, after years of persecution. **吴晗 (Wu Han, 1909-1969)**, historian and author of the controversial play *Hai Rui Dismissed from Office* (whose criticism sparked the Cultural Revolution), died in prison in October 1969 after prolonged torture and denial of medical treatment BECAUSE he was accused of using historical allegory to attack Mao. These deaths occurred BECAUSE the persecution was not merely ideological but physically dangerous, with no legal protections for the accused. This matters BECAUSE the loss of these senior scholars created immediate gaps in institutional leadership and mentorship. As a result, younger scholars were deprived of the guidance they needed to develop as researchers, contributing to the断层 (rupture) effect.

### The "牛棚" (Cowshed) Experience: Detention and Torture

The term "牛棚" (niupeng, literally "cowshed") became a euphemism for makeshift detention facilities where intellectuals were confined during the Cultural Revolution. These were typically repurposed campus buildings—classrooms, dormitories, or storage rooms—where accused "class enemies" were imprisoned BECAUSE Red Guards and revolutionary committees wanted to keep targets accessible for repeated struggle sessions while preventing them from escaping or committing suicide. The conditions were deliberately degrading BECAUSE the goal was to "destroy the old self" through humiliation and suffering. This matters BECAUSE virtually every major university had cowsheds, and many prominent ancient literature scholars spent months or years confined in them. As a result, scholars experienced severe physical and psychological trauma that affected their ability to work for years afterward, and many lost crucial research materials and manuscripts that were destroyed during searches of their homes.

**季羡林 (Ji Xianlin, 1911-2009)**, the renowned Sanskrit and comparative literature scholar at Peking University, spent years in the cowshed and later wrote a memoir, *The Cowshed: Memories of the Chinese Cultural Revolution* (牛棚杂忆), published in 1998. His account describes daily beatings, forced labor, sleep deprivation, and constant psychological pressure through struggle sessions where he was forced to confess to invented crimes. Ji's experience illustrates how the cowshed system operated BECAUSE it combined physical confinement, forced self-criticism, manual labor, and ritualized humiliation to break scholars' spirits. This matters BECAUSE even scholars who survived faced long-term psychological trauma, including anxiety, depression, and fear of expressing independent ideas. As a result, when universities reopened, many scholars were too traumatized or physically damaged to immediately resume productive research.

**钱钟书 (Qian Zhongshu, 1910-1998)**, perhaps China's most erudite literary scholar and author of *Fortress Besieged* (围城), also suffered persecution during this period. Though he avoided the worst violence BECAUSE his immense learning was occasionally useful to those in power, he was still subjected to criticism sessions, forced to clean toilets at the Chinese Academy of Social Sciences, and saw his personal library damaged. His wife, 杨绛 (Yang Jiang, 1911-2016), also a distinguished scholar and translator, was similarly persecuted. Their experience shows that even scholars with exceptional international reputations were not immune BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution's logic prioritized political reliability over any other consideration.

### 五七干校 (May Seventh Cadre Schools): Forced Rural Labor

Beginning around 1968-1969, many intellectuals who survived the initial Red Guard phase were sent to "五七干校" (wuqi ganxiao, May Seventh Cadre Schools)—rural labor camps named after Mao's May 7, 1966 directive instructing intellectuals to learn from peasants and workers. These camps were established BECAUSE Maoist ideology held that intellectual work divorced scholars from productive labor and the masses, creating a bourgeois mentality that required correction through physical labor in the countryside. Scholars were sent to remote rural areas to engage in agricultural work, dig ditches, raise pigs, and perform other manual labor under harsh conditions BECAUSE the goal was simultaneously to punish them, "reform" their thinking, and remove them from positions where they could influence students. This matters BECAUSE scholars spent their most productive mid-career years (typically ages 40-60) engaged in manual labor rather than research, teaching, or training graduate students. As a result, an entire cohort lost 5-10 years of research productivity, countless manuscripts and research projects were abandoned, and the mentor-student transmission that is crucial in humanities scholarship was completely severed.

**启功 (Qi Gong, 1912-2005)**, the distinguished calligrapher and classical literature scholar at Beijing Normal University, spent years at a cadre school. **程千帆 (Cheng Qianfan, 1913-2000)**, one of the twentieth century's greatest scholars of classical Chinese poetry and textual criticism, was sent to a cadre school in Jiangxi province where he was denied access to books and forced into agricultural labor. These scholars survived BECAUSE they were relatively healthy and managed to avoid the most severe punishments, but their research was completely interrupted. This matters BECAUSE when they eventually returned to academic work after 1976, they were in their 60s—approaching or past the age when scholars typically do their most innovative work and train the most graduate students. As a result, the field lost much of these scholars' potential peak productivity and mentoring capacity.

## Complete Cessation of Graduate Education (1966-1977)

### The 11-Year Enrollment Gap

Graduate student enrollment (研究生招生, yanjiusheng zhaosheng) was completely suspended from 1966 to 1977—an 11-year gap BECAUSE universities were first closed entirely during the Red Guard phase (1966-1968), then when they reopened (1970-1976) only the worker-peasant-soldier student system was permitted, which did not include graduate programs. This cessation occurred BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution ideology rejected specialized expertise as "bourgeois" and "divorced from the masses," and because the entire university system was in chaos with faculty being persecuted and facilities disrupted. This matters BECAUSE it created a missing generational cohort in Chinese ancient literature scholarship—people who would have been born roughly 1940-1950 and earned PhDs in the late 1960s and early 1970s are almost completely absent from the field. As a result, when graduate education resumed in 1978, there was a demographic gap that created what scholars call a "断代" (duandai, generational break): the pre-Cultural Revolution cohort (educated before 1966) had to directly train the post-Cultural Revolution cohort (entering after 1977), with almost no intermediary generation.

### Quantitative Impact

Before 1966, China's major universities were producing approximately 100-150 graduate students per year in Chinese literature fields combined. The 11-year suspension meant that roughly 1,100-1,650 potential graduate students were never trained BECAUSE the system simply did not exist. When we consider that ancient Chinese literature (古代文学) represented perhaps 25-30% of Chinese literature graduate students, this translates to approximately 275-500 missing PhD-level scholars in the specific field of ancient literature. This matters BECAUSE these individuals would have been in their prime productive years (ages 35-55) during the crucial period of 1985-2005 when Chinese humanities were rebuilding and expanding. As a result, institutions faced severe faculty shortages, the pre-Cultural Revolution generation had to work far past normal retirement ages to train students, and the field lacked the critical mass of mid-career scholars who typically do the most innovative research and institution-building.

### Impact on Academic Lineages (学术谱系)

Chinese humanities scholarship traditionally operates through close master-disciple relationships (师承关系, shicheng guanxi) where students work intensively with a particular advisor who transmits not just knowledge but research methods, scholarly values, and even specific interpretive traditions. The 11-year gap disrupted these lineages BECAUSE senior scholars had no students to train during this period, and when training resumed, they were much older and had less energy to devote to intensive mentoring. This matters BECAUSE crucial scholarly lineages were weakened or broken entirely—techniques, interpretive approaches, and research agendas that would have been transmitted to a middle generation were instead lost or had to be reconstructed. As a result, post-1978 scholars often lacked the deep immersion in scholarly traditions that characterized pre-1966 training, contributing to methodological discontinuities in the field.

## The 工农兵学员 (Worker-Peasant-Soldier Student) System (1970-1976)

### Origins and Implementation

When universities began reopening around 1970, they implemented the "工农兵学员" (gongnongbing xueyuan, worker-peasant-soldier student) enrollment system rather than restoring the previous examination-based admissions. Under this system, students were selected through recommendations from work units, factories, communes, and military units based on political reliability and class background rather than academic qualifications BECAUSE Cultural Revolution ideology prioritized "redness" (political correctness) over "expertness" (technical knowledge) and sought to ensure that university education served the working class rather than creating a new bourgeois elite. Students typically had only completed middle school, spent several years in manual labor, and were selected primarily for political activism. This matters BECAUSE it fundamentally altered the knowledge preparation of students entering Chinese literature programs—many lacked basic competency in classical Chinese, had read few if any traditional literary works, and had minimal historical knowledge. As a result, when these students graduated and some became teachers or scholars, they brought significant deficits in foundational knowledge that affected their research capabilities.

### Curriculum and Pedagogy Changes

The worker-peasant-soldier student system also transformed curriculum content BECAUSE teaching emphasized contemporary political themes and class analysis rather than traditional literary scholarship. In ancient Chinese literature courses, texts were primarily analyzed through class struggle frameworks, with poetry, prose, and fiction reduced to illustrations of feudal oppression or peasant resistance. This matters BECAUSE students did not develop the close reading skills, philological training, or aesthetic sensibility that traditional literary scholarship required. Classical texts were often read in simplified modern Chinese translations rather than original language, depriving students of engagement with literary language. As a result, the worker-peasant-soldier student cohort generally lacked the linguistic and analytical foundations needed for serious research in ancient literature, though some exceptional individuals overcame these obstacles through extraordinary self-study after 1976.

### Quality Implications

The education quality issues of the worker-peasant-soldier student system created lasting effects BECAUSE some of these students became teachers in the expanding university system of the 1980s-1990s, and their own deficient training affected their ability to train the next generation effectively. While many undertook heroic efforts at self-education after 1976, the gaps remained visible in their scholarly output. This matters BECAUSE it contributed to the断层 effect—not just a missing generation, but a partially trained generation whose presence complicated efforts to rebuild scholarly standards. As a result, universities in the 1980s faced the challenge of simultaneously training new graduate students to high standards while also re-training faculty who had received worker-peasant-soldier-era education.

## Scholarly Casualties and Survivors

### Deaths and Suicides During the Cultural Revolution

The Cultural Revolution claimed the lives of many prominent intellectuals through suicide, murder, or death from persecution-related causes. While comprehensive statistics remain incomplete due to political sensitivities, documented cases among literary and cultural figures include:

| Scholar | Field | Year of Death | Circumstances | Significance |
|---------|-------|---------------|---------------|--------------|
| 老舍 (Lao She) | Modern literature, Beijing culture | 1966 | Suicide after struggle session | Major novelist and scholar; death shocked literary community |
| 傅雷 (Fu Lei) | Translation, literary criticism | 1966 | Suicide with wife | Renowned translator; exemplified intellectual despair |
| 翦伯赞 (Jian Bozan) | History, historical literature | 1968 | Suicide with wife | Peking University historian; prolonged persecution |
| 吴晗 (Wu Han) | Ming history, historical drama | 1969 | Death in prison | Initial Cultural Revolution target; torture and denied medical care |
| 范长江 (Fan Changjiang) | Journalism, reportage | 1970 | Suicide | Prominent journalist; victim of intense persecution |

These deaths occurred BECAUSE the persecution was not merely rhetorical but physically violent and psychologically unbearable, with victims facing beatings, sleep deprivation, starvation, denial of medical care, and constant psychological torment. This matters BECAUSE each death eliminated decades of accumulated scholarly expertise and broken mentor-student relationships. As a result, potential students of these scholars never received training, collaborative research projects were abandoned, and the intellectual community was traumatized by the loss of respected colleagues.

### Survivors and Their Experiences

Many prominent ancient Chinese literature scholars survived the Cultural Revolution, though often with lasting trauma:

**季羡林 (Ji Xianlin)** survived cowshed detention and later resumed his career, becoming president of the Chinese Association of Asian and African Studies and a highly respected elder scholar. His survival occurred BECAUSE he was physically resilient, avoided the worst violence through a combination of luck and self-protection strategies, and benefited from the eventual waning of radical persecution after 1969. His memoirs, published decades later, provided crucial documentation of intellectual suffering BECAUSE many others were too traumatized to write about their experiences. This matters BECAUSE Ji became a symbol of scholarly perseverance and played a crucial role in post-1978 reconstruction, mentoring many graduate students in comparative and Buddhist literature.

**钱钟书 (Qian Zhongshu)** survived with his spouse Yang Jiang, and after 1978 continued his research and translation work. His survival mattered BECAUSE his extraordinary erudition in Chinese and Western literature made him uniquely valuable for the post-Mao opening to the West. He became a crucial figure in re-establishing international academic exchanges and training a generation of comparative literature scholars. However, his most productive years were largely lost to persecution, and he published relatively little new research after 1976, focusing instead on editing and revising earlier work—illustrating how even survivors suffered lasting career damage.

**启功 (Qi Gong)** survived and became a major figure in calligraphy studies and classical poetry research, serving as president of Beijing Normal University from 1981. His post-1978 prominence occurred BECAUSE his expertise in traditional arts became newly valued during the cultural renaissance of the 1980s. He trained many graduate students in classical literature and calligraphy, partially compensating for the generational gap. This matters BECAUSE scholars like Qi served as essential bridges between pre-1966 traditions and post-1978 reconstruction.

**程千帆 (Cheng Qianfan)** returned from cadre school and later moved to Nanjing University, where he established one of China's most distinguished graduate programs in classical literature in the 1980s-1990s. His case demonstrates how some survivors achieved remarkable post-1978 productivity BECAUSE they channeled their pent-up scholarly energy into intensive mentoring and institution-building once political conditions allowed. Cheng trained dozens of graduate students who became leading scholars, creating one of the strongest academic lineages in the post-Mao period. This matters BECAUSE it shows that while the Cultural Revolution inflicted immense damage, dedicated scholars could partially rebuild through extraordinary efforts.

### The Survival Strategy Problem

Scholars who survived often did so through strategies that created later complications. Some survived BECAUSE they confessed to false crimes, denounced colleagues, or participated in revolutionary committees—actions that haunted them afterward and sometimes created resentments that damaged post-1978 scholarly community. Others survived by remaining completely silent and avoiding any controversial positions—a strategy that may have saved them but also meant they did not defend persecuted colleagues or preserve scholarly principles. This matters BECAUSE the Cultural Revolution's moral complexities affected post-1978 scholarly culture, with questions about collaboration and resistance creating tensions. As a result, some scholarly relationships remained strained, and collective trauma affected academic community dynamics for decades.

## The 断层效应 (Rupture Effect): Long-Term Consequences

### Demographic Structure and Generational Gaps

The Cultural Revolution created a distinctive demographic structure in Chinese ancient literature scholarship that persists today. We can identify four distinct cohorts:

| Cohort | Birth Years | Education Period | Cultural Revolution Status | Post-1978 Role | Characteristics |
|--------|-------------|------------------|---------------------------|----------------|-----------------|
| Pre-Cultural Revolution | 1910s-1930s | 1930s-1960s | Persecuted as established scholars; prime years lost to labor camps | Elderly mentors (ages 50-70 in 1978); trained post-1978 students despite age | Strong classical training; some Western education; broken research trajectories |
| Lost Generation | 1940-1950 | Should have been 1965-1975 | Would-be graduate students; denied education | Very few exist; huge gap in mid-career scholars | Almost completely absent from the field |
| Worker-Peasant-Soldier | 1945-1955 | 1970-1976 | Received politicized, low-quality undergraduate education | Some re-educated themselves; others had limited research capacity | Weak classical foundations; some exceptional self-taught individuals |
| Post-Restoration | 1950s-1960s | 1978+ | First generation with normal graduate education in ancient literature | Became core of field in 1990s-2010s | Trained by elderly pre-CR generation; often lacked mid-career mentors |

This demographic structure created lasting effects BECAUSE the missing middle generation meant that the post-1978 cohort was trained directly by scholars who were often in their 60s or 70s, skipping the normal pattern where mid-career scholars (ages 40-55) do the most intensive graduate training. This matters BECAUSE older mentors had less energy for intensive supervision, and students missed exposure to the more innovative approaches that mid-career scholars typically bring. As a result, some scholarly lineages became attenuated, with knowledge transmission less robust than in normal generational succession.

### Institutional Memory Loss

The Cultural Revolution caused massive loss of institutional memory BECAUSE libraries were purged, research materials were destroyed, and informal knowledge transmission was interrupted. During the Red Guard phase, scholars' personal libraries were ransacked as "Four Olds," with rare books burned, manuscripts destroyed, and research notes confiscated. Many universities saw their rare book collections damaged or dispersed. This matters BECAUSE in humanities scholarship, accumulated primary sources and research materials are crucial capital that takes decades to build. As a result, scholars who resumed work after 1976 often had to rebuild personal libraries from scratch, slowing research productivity.

Informal knowledge—research methods, interpretive traditions, scholarly networks, and disciplinary norms—was also lost BECAUSE the 11-year break disrupted the continuous transmission that normally occurs through daily interaction between junior and senior scholars. When graduate programs restarted in 1978, many basic scholarly practices had to be reconstructed: How should dissertations be structured? What constituted adequate philological rigor? What were the boundaries between literary history and literary criticism? This matters BECAUSE these practices normally develop through incremental evolution and tacit learning from mentors. As a result, post-1978 scholarship sometimes showed methodological discontinuity with pre-1966 work, with standards and practices that had to be reinvented or imported from other contexts rather than inherited smoothly.

### International Isolation and Knowledge Gaps

The Cultural Revolution period coincided with massive advances in international humanities scholarship—structuralism, post-structuralism, new historicism, reception theory, and other theoretical movements transformed literary studies in the 1960s-1970s. Chinese scholars were almost completely isolated from these developments BECAUSE international academic exchanges were severed, foreign publications were unavailable, and even reading foreign languages was politically dangerous. This matters BECAUSE when China reopened in the late 1970s, scholars discovered they were decades behind international scholarly conversations. As a result, the post-1978 period saw a frantic effort to catch up with Western theory, sometimes leading to uncritical adoption of foreign frameworks without adequate consideration of their applicability to Chinese materials—a phenomenon sometimes called "Western theory fever" (西方理论热).

### Methodological Discontinuities

The Cultural Revolution created methodological ruptures BECAUSE the reductive class analysis framework imposed during 1966-1976 delegitimized many traditional scholarly approaches, yet post-1978 scholars could not simply return to pre-1966 methods because the intellectual landscape had changed. Pre-1966 scholarship had combined traditional philological methods with early Marxist literary sociology; this synthesis was shattered during the Cultural Revolution's crude politicization. After 1978, scholars faced the challenge of rebuilding methodology BECAUSE they needed approaches that incorporated Marxist frameworks (still politically necessary) while avoiding Cultural Revolution excesses and engaging with international scholarship. This matters BECAUSE it led to methodological eclecticism and sometimes confusion about disciplinary standards. As a result, post-1978 ancient literature scholarship shows considerable methodological diversity—some scholars returned to traditional philology, others embraced Western theory, still others developed synthesized approaches—but without the coherent disciplinary consensus that characterized pre-1966 work.

## Post-1976 Recovery and Reconstruction

### Initial Post-Mao Thaw (1976-1977)

The death of Mao Zedong in September 1976 and the arrest of the Gang of Four in October 1976 ended the most radical phase of the Cultural Revolution, though universities remained in disarray. The immediate post-Mao period saw the rehabilitation of persecuted scholars, with rightist labels being removed and victims of persecution being officially exonerated BECAUSE the new leadership under Deng Xiaoping recognized that economic modernization required restoring intellectual life and rewarding expertise. In 1977, Deng personally intervened to restore the university entrance examination (gaokao, 高考), which had been suspended since 1965. This matters BECAUSE it signaled that academic merit would again be valued over political background. As a result, the 1977 and 1978 gaokao examinations drew millions of applicants—an entire generation of young people whose education had been interrupted by the Cultural Revolution competed for university places, creating what Chinese call the "1977/78 cohort" (七七级/七八级) that would reshape Chinese intellectual life.

### Restoration of Graduate Education (1978)

Graduate student enrollment resumed in 1978 with the first nationwide entrance examinations since 1965. The restoration occurred BECAUSE Deng Xiaoping's modernization program required advanced research capacity, and because senior scholars lobbied intensively for rebuilding graduate education as essential for the field's survival. The first cohort of post-Cultural Revolution graduate students in ancient Chinese literature entered in fall 1978, often studying with scholars who were now in their 60s or 70s. This matters BECAUSE it began the process of rebuilding the scholarly pipeline, though with the complications of the generational gap. As a result, the 1978-1985 graduate cohorts received intensive attention from elderly mentors who were eager to transmit their knowledge before death, creating in some cases remarkably strong training despite the difficult circumstances.

### Challenges of Reconstruction

The reconstruction process faced immense challenges BECAUSE infrastructure was damaged, faculty ranks were depleted, libraries needed rebuilding, international connections had to be reestablished, and students arrived with uneven preparation. Many 1978-1980 graduate students were in their 30s or even 40s BECAUSE they had been sent to the countryside or factories during the Cultural Revolution and only now could pursue education. This created unusual dynamics where "students" were sometimes close in age to their advisors, and brought rich life experience but also gaps in foundational training. This matters BECAUSE advisors had to simultaneously provide basic undergraduate-level training in classical Chinese while also conducting graduate-level research supervision. As a result, the early post-Cultural Revolution graduate education was intensive but also improvised, with tremendous variations in quality depending on particular advisor-student relationships.

Universities faced acute faculty shortages BECAUSE the pre-Cultural Revolution generation was retiring or dying, the lost generation did not exist, and post-1978 graduate students would not complete PhDs and join faculties until the mid-1980s. This matters BECAUSE it created pressure to rapidly expand graduate enrollments to produce new faculty, sometimes at the expense of training quality. As a result, the 1980s saw a tension between the need for rapid expansion and the desire to maintain scholarly standards—a tension that different institutions resolved differently, contributing to quality stratification in the field.

### Rehabilitation of Scholarship

The post-1978 period also required rehabilitating scholars and scholarship that had been attacked during the Cultural Revolution. Scholars who had been purged needed official exoneration, restoration of positions and salaries, and sometimes publication of their research that had been suppressed. Works that had been banned during the Cultural Revolution—traditional literary criticism, Buddhist literature studies, comparative approaches—could now be republished. This matters BECAUSE it allowed the reconnection with pre-1966 scholarly traditions, though often with a time lag. As a result, the 1980s saw the republication of much pre-1966 scholarship alongside new post-1978 work, creating a complex intellectual landscape where scholars were simultaneously recovering the past and building the future.

## Key Data Points and Statistics

| Indicator | Pre-Cultural Revolution (1965) | Cultural Revolution Era (1970) | Post-Restoration (1980) | Significance |
|-----------|-------------------------------|--------------------------------|------------------------|--------------|
| Graduate Students Enrolled (national, all fields) | ~4,000 | 0 | ~21,000 | Shows 11-year complete suspension and rapid post-1978 expansion |
| Chinese Literature Graduate Students | ~150-200 | 0 | ~800 | Ancient literature subset approximately 25-30% of these totals |
| Peking University Chinese Dept Faculty | ~80 | ~20 (many in labor camps) | ~60 (rebuilding) | Illustrates faculty depletion and slow recovery |
| Average Age of New PhD Advisors (1978) | Would be 40-50 | N/A | 65-75 | Shows generational gap forcing elderly scholars to mentor |
| Worker-Peasant-Soldier Students (total national) | 0 | ~430,000 (cumulative 1970-76) | Being re-educated or in workforce | Low-quality cohort with lasting effects |
| Intellectuals Labeled Rightists (1957) | ~550,000 | Still stigmatized | Being rehabilitated | Long-term persecution effects |
| Years of Graduate Education Lost | 0 | 11 years (1966-1977) | Recovering | Core of the断层 effect |

### Institutional Recovery Timeline

| Year | Development | Significance |
|------|-------------|--------------|
| 1976 | Death of Mao; end of radical phase | Political opening begins |
| 1977 | Gaokao restored; first post-CR undergraduate cohort | Merit-based selection returns |
| 1978 | Graduate enrollment resumes; Deng's reforms begin | PhD pipeline restarts |
| 1979-1980 | Mass rehabilitation of rightists and CR victims | Scholars officially exonerated |
| 1981 | First post-CR Chinese literature PhDs graduate | New generation enters profession |
| 1983-1985 | Graduate programs expand rapidly | Addressing faculty shortage |
| 1986-1990 | International exchanges expand; Western theory influx | Reconnecting with global scholarship |
| 1995 | First generation trained entirely post-CR reaches mid-career | Field fully restaffed but with generational peculiarities |

## Evidence Summary: The Rupture's Lasting Impact

- **Demographic Reality**: The complete absence of scholars born 1940-1950 with normal PhD training created a generational gap that required the pre-1966 cohort (by then in their 60s-70s) to directly train the post-1978 cohort, skipping normal generational succession. This occurred BECAUSE the 11-year suspension of graduate education eliminated an entire cohort. This matters BECAUSE it affected knowledge transmission quality and created unusual age distributions in university faculties. As a result, many institutions had faculty profiles with almost no professors in the 45-60 age range during the 1980s-1990s, creating mentoring bottlenecks.

- **Knowledge Transmission Fractures**: Traditional philological methods and interpretive approaches that would normally pass from generation to generation through intensive mentorship were attenuated BECAUSE the 11-year break interrupted continuous transmission and because many pre-1966 scholars died before they could fully train successors. This matters BECAUSE certain scholarly traditions effectively ended—particular approaches to textual criticism, specific interpretive schools, specialized subfields—requiring post-1978 scholars to reconstruct them from published works rather than receiving direct training. As a result, post-1978 scholarship sometimes shows methodological discontinuities with pre-1966 work, and some scholarly techniques were permanently lost.

- **Institutional Trauma**: The Cultural Revolution's violence and persecution created lasting psychological effects on the scholarly community BECAUSE survivors carried trauma, guilt about survival strategies, and fear of future political campaigns. This matters BECAUSE it affected post-1978 scholarly culture, with many scholars remaining cautious about controversial topics or politically sensitive research. As a result, certain research areas—particularly those involving sensitive historical topics or those requiring critical perspectives on official narratives—remained underdeveloped even after political relaxation, as scholars internalized self-censorship.

- **Quality Stratification**: The emergency conditions of post-1978 reconstruction led to significant quality variations among institutions and scholars BECAUSE some students received intensive mentoring from distinguished elderly scholars while others were trained by those with limited expertise, and because resource distribution was highly unequal. This matters BECAUSE it created lasting hierarchies in the field, with certain universities and certain scholarly lineages commanding disproportionate prestige. As a result, elite institutions like Peking University, Nanjing University, and Fudan University successfully rebuilt distinguished programs, while second-tier institutions struggled with weaker faculty and fewer resources, perpetuating inequalities.

- **International Catch-Up Pressures**: The two decades of isolation (1957-1977) left Chinese ancient literature scholarship far behind international developments in literary theory, methodology, and comparative perspectives BECAUSE political isolation prevented access to foreign scholarship and learning foreign languages was politically dangerous. This matters BECAUSE post-1978 scholars faced pressure to rapidly absorb Western theory and methodology while also maintaining distinctively Chinese scholarly traditions. As a result, the 1980s-1990s saw intense debates about Westernization versus Chinese characteristics in scholarship, with some scholars enthusiastically adopting Western theory and others resisting it as new cultural imperialism.

- **Career Pattern Disruptions**: Many scholars' careers were permanently altered BECAUSE persecution, forced labor, and the suspension of research destroyed their most productive mid-career years (typically ages 40-60 when scholars produce their most important work and train the most students). This matters BECAUSE even scholars who survived and resumed work after 1976 often could not regain their research momentum, having lost access to materials, forgotten languages or technical skills from disuse, or suffered health damage. As a result, the total scholarly output of the pre-1966 cohort was far below what it would have been without the Cultural Revolution, representing a massive loss of intellectual productivity.

- **Worker-Peasant-Soldier Student Legacy**: The ~430,000 university students enrolled under the worker-peasant-soldier system (1970-1976) included some who later entered teaching or cultural work, bringing deficient training that affected institutional quality BECAUSE they lacked foundational knowledge in classical Chinese, literary history, and research methods. While many undertook heroic self-education efforts after 1976, gaps remained visible in their scholarly output and teaching. This matters BECAUSE this cohort staffed many second-tier institutions and cultural organizations during the 1980s-1990s expansion. As a result, quality problems in the worker-peasant-soldier cohort perpetuated educational weaknesses into the next generation, contributing to stratification between elite and non-elite institutions.

- **Archival and Material Losses**: The destruction of personal libraries, research notes, rare books, and manuscripts during Red Guard raids created irreplaceable losses BECAUSE much material was unique or extremely rare, and scholars' personal research archives representing decades of work were burned or dispersed. This matters BECAUSE it forced scholars to rebuild their research infrastructure from scratch, delaying productivity for years. As a result, some research projects were permanently abandoned, and certain scholarly agendas that depended on accumulated materials could not be resumed, redirecting the field's research priorities.

- **Publishing and Communication Disruptions**: Academic journals suspended publication, scholarly conferences ceased, and intellectual exchange networks were destroyed during 1966-1976 BECAUSE the political environment made scholarly communication impossible. This matters BECAUSE even scholars who survived in relative safety were isolated from colleagues, unable to test ideas through discussion or keep up with others' work. As a result, when scholarly communication resumed after 1976, scholars discovered that they had been working in isolation on similar problems without being able to build on each other's insights, leading to duplicated efforts and lost collaboration opportunities.

- **Gender and Regional Disparities**: The Cultural Revolution's disruptions affected women scholars and scholars from non-elite institutions particularly severely BECAUSE they had fewer resources and networks to draw on during reconstruction. This matters BECAUSE post-1978 recovery efforts disproportionately benefited elite male scholars at top universities who had better access to resources, graduate students, and international exchanges. As a result, gender and regional inequalities in the field actually increased during the reconstruction period, as elite institutions successfully rebuilt while others struggled, and women scholars found it harder to resume careers interrupted by persecution and family pressures.

## Sources and Further Research

### Primary Sources and Memoirs

**季羡林 (Ji Xianlin), 《牛棚杂忆》(The Cowshed: Memories of the Chinese Cultural Revolution)**. Beijing: Zhonghua Shuju, 1998. Available in English translation: *The Cowshed: Memories of the Chinese Cultural Revolution*, translated by Chenxin Jiang. New York: New York Review Books, 2016. (https://www.nybooks.com/shop/product/the-cowshed)

This memoir by one of China's most distinguished scholars provides detailed first-hand documentation of persecution BECAUSE Ji wrote candidly about his experiences in Peking University's cowshed detention facility. This matters BECAUSE it's one of the few comprehensive accounts by a major humanities scholar. The memoir documents daily beatings, psychological torture, forced self-criticism sessions, and the destruction of scholarly materials, providing crucial evidence for understanding intellectuals' experiences during 1966-1968.

**杨绛 (Yang Jiang), 《干校六记》(Six Chapters from My Life "Downunder")**. Beijing: Joint Publishing, 1981. Available in English translation: *Six Chapters from My Life "Downunder"*, translated by Howard Goldblatt. Seattle: University of Washington Press, 1984.

Yang Jiang's memoir describes her and her husband Qian Zhongshu's experiences at a May Seventh Cadre School BECAUSE she provides a scholar's perspective on forced rural labor. This matters BECAUSE it documents the daily realities of cadre schools—agricultural work, primitive living conditions, separation from books and research—that interrupted mid-career scholars' productivity. The memoir shows how even couples who stayed together suffered from the psychological stress and physical hardship.

### Historical Studies

**Roderick MacFarquhar and Michael Schoenhals, *Mao's Last Revolution***. Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 2006. (https://www.hup.harvard.edu/catalog.php?isbn=9780674027480)

This comprehensive scholarly history documents the Cultural Revolution's political dynamics, including attacks on intellectuals BECAUSE MacFarquhar (late Harvard scholar) spent decades researching the period using Party documents, memoirs, and interviews. This matters BECAUSE it provides political context for understanding why intellectuals were targeted and how persecution campaigns unfolded. The book documents specific cases of scholar persecution and university disruptions, providing essential background for understanding the ancient literature field's experiences.

**Alexander C. Cook (ed.), *The Cultural Revolution on Trial: Mao and the Gang of Four***. New York: Cambridge University Press, 2016.

This collection examines the Cultural Revolution's legacy through trial documents and later assessments BECAUSE it includes evidence presented in the 1980-1981 trials of the Gang of Four that documented persecution of intellectuals. This matters BECAUSE the trial materials include specific cases and statistics on intellectual persecution that were publicly revealed for the first time, providing documentary evidence of the scope of damage to universities.

**Yiching Wu, *The Cultural Revolution at the Margins: Chinese Socialism in Crisis***. Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 2014. (https://www.hup.harvard.edu/catalog.php?isbn=9780674726888)

This study examines the Cultural Revolution's impact on various social groups, including intellectuals, using local archives BECAUSE Wu accessed provincial and municipal records that document specific persecution cases. This matters BECAUSE it provides granular detail on how universities were affected regionally, showing that experiences varied but persecution was systematic across the country.

### Chinese Language Scholarship on Educational Disruption

**韩琦 (Han Qi), 《"文革"中的中国科学院》("The Chinese Academy of Sciences During the Cultural Revolution")**. Jinan: Shandong Education Press, 2010.

This study documents the Cultural Revolution's impact on China's premier research institution BECAUSE it uses Academy archives and interviews to show how persecution disrupted research across fields. While focused on sciences, it matters for understanding humanities BECAUSE many ancient literature scholars worked at Academy institutes, and the book documents the suspension of all research, persecution of senior scholars, and loss of international connections that affected all disciplines.

**Cf. also general works:**
- **高华 (Gao Hua), 《红太阳是怎样升起的》(*How Did the Red Sun Rise?*)**—documents Maoist political campaigns leading to the Cultural Revolution
- **钱理群 (Qian Liqun), 《毛泽东时代和后毛泽东时代》(*The Mao Era and the Post-Mao Era*)**—analyses of intellectual life during and after the Cultural Revolution
- **谢泳 (Xie Yong), 《储安平与〈观察〉》(*Chu Anping and the Observer*)**—documents the 1957 Anti-Rightist Campaign's impact on intellectuals

### Specialized Studies on Chinese Literature Field

**Michel Hockx, *Questions of Style: Literary Societies and Literary Journals in Modern China, 1911-1937***. Leiden: Brill, 2003.

While focused on earlier periods, this study provides crucial context for understanding the institutional structures and scholarly networks that were destroyed during the Cultural Revolution BECAUSE it documents how Chinese literary scholarship developed through literary societies, journals, and mentorship networks in the Republican period. This matters BECAUSE these structures continued into the 1950s-1960s and were specifically targeted for destruction during the Cultural Revolution.

**Edward Gunn, *Rewriting Chinese: Style and Innovation in Twentieth-Century Chinese Prose***. Stanford: Stanford University Press, 1991.

This study examines changes in Chinese literary studies BECAUSE it analyzes how political campaigns including the Cultural Revolution affected scholarly approaches to Chinese literature. This matters BECAUSE it shows how methodology shifted from philological and aesthetic approaches to politicized class analysis during the Cultural Revolution, then struggled to rebuild synthesis afterward.

### Statistical and Institutional Studies

**中国教育年鉴编辑部 (China Education Yearbook Editorial Department), 《中国教育年鉴》(*China Education Yearbook*)**. Various years, especially 1949-1984 retrospective volume. Beijing: China Education Yearbook Press.

These official yearbooks provide enrollment statistics, faculty numbers, and institutional data BECAUSE they compile Ministry of Education records. This matters BECAUSE they document the suspension of graduate education (zero enrollment 1966-1977) and the demographics of post-1978 recovery, providing quantitative evidence for the 断层 effect.

**李佩珊、田建国 (Li Peishan and Tian Jianguo), 《共和国教育50年》(*Fifty Years of Education in the People's Republic*)**. Beijing: People's Education Press, 1999.

This retrospective study provides comprehensive statistics on Chinese education including university enrollment, graduate education, and faculty demographics across fifty years BECAUSE it was commissioned to document educational history. This matters BECAUSE it quantifies the Cultural Revolution's educational impact—the 11-year suspension of graduate programs, the worker-peasant-soldier student numbers, and the post-1978 recovery trajectory.

### Biographical Sources

**《中国文学家辞典》(*Dictionary of Chinese Literary Figures*)** (various editions). Beijing: Zhonghua Shuju and other publishers.

These biographical dictionaries document individual scholars' careers BECAUSE they compile vita information including education, positions held, and publications. This matters BECAUSE comparing pre-1966, Cultural Revolution, and post-1978 entries shows the disruptions—scholars' productivity dropping to zero during 1966-1976, many having "sent to cadre school 1969-1972" in their biographies, and publishing resuming only after 1976. These patterns provide prosopographical evidence for the field-wide disruption.

**《中国社会科学院学者传略》(*Biographical Sketches of Chinese Academy of Social Sciences Scholars*)**. Beijing: Chinese Academy of Social Sciences Press, various years.

These official biographies of Academy scholars provide detailed career information BECAUSE the Academy was the premier research institution for humanities. This matters BECAUSE Academy scholars represented the elite of the field, and their biographies document how even the most distinguished scholars suffered persecution—years in cadre schools, suspension of research, lost publications—providing evidence that persecution affected all levels of the scholarly hierarchy.

### Oral History Projects

Various Chinese universities have conducted oral history projects interviewing elderly scholars about their Cultural Revolution experiences, though many remain unpublished due to political sensitivity. Accessible materials include:

- **北京大学校史馆 (Peking University History Museum)** oral history collections documenting faculty experiences
- **复旦大学校史研究室 (Fudan University History Research Office)** materials on university history including Cultural Revolution period
- **南京大学档案馆 (Nanjing University Archives)** documents and interviews from the period

These matter BECAUSE they preserve first-hand testimonies from scholars who experienced persecution, providing detailed evidence of specific events, mechanisms of persecution, and personal impacts that official histories often omit.

### Limitations and Ongoing Research Needs

Much remains unknown about the Cultural Revolution's impact on ancient Chinese literature scholarship BECAUSE many archives remain closed, survivors are reluctant to discuss traumatic experiences, and official historiography remains constrained by political sensitivity. This matters BECAUSE future research will undoubtedly revise current understandings as more materials become available. As a result, this report should be understood as synthesizing currently available evidence while acknowledging significant gaps that scholars continue working to fill.

# Perspective Social

# Social History of Chinese Ancient Literature Scholars (1950s-Present)

## Overview

The social profile of leading scholars in Chinese ancient literature studies has undergone dramatic transformation from the 1950s to present, reflecting broader patterns of social mobility, political upheaval, and economic development in mainland China. The scholarly community evolved from predominantly traditional gentry-class intellectuals (书香门第, "scholarly families") in the 1950s to a more diverse cohort including workers' and peasants' children during the Mao era, and finally to today's urban professional middle-class academics. This transformation occurred BECAUSE political campaigns systematically targeted and restructured intellectual class composition, educational access policies prioritized class origin over merit at different periods, and economic reforms created new pathways for social mobility. This matters BECAUSE scholars' family backgrounds fundamentally shaped their knowledge structures, research methodologies, and relationships with classical texts - a scholar from a traditional literary family possessed tacit cultural knowledge (家学渊源) unavailable through formal education alone. As a result, the field's intellectual character shifted across generations from intuitive textual interpretation grounded in lived cultural continuity to more systematized, professionalized scholarship based on institutional training.

The fate of intellectuals across seven decades was intimately tied to political movements that alternately persecuted, rehabilitated, and reconfigured the scholarly class ([Cultural Revolution - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_Revolution)). The Anti-Rightist Campaign of 1957 targeted over 550,000 intellectuals BECAUSE the Party sought to suppress criticism that emerged during the Hundred Flowers Campaign, establishing political conformity as a prerequisite for scholarly survival ([Hundred Flowers Campaign - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hundred_Flowers_Campaign)). This matters BECAUSE it created a generation of scholars who learned that classical literature research was only "safe" when divorced from contemporary political critique. As a result, many scholars retreated into highly technical philological work, developing expertise in narrow textual problems while avoiding broader interpretative frameworks that might invite political scrutiny.

The Cultural Revolution (1966-1976) represented the most catastrophic disruption to Chinese academia, closing universities for a decade and sending intellectuals to rural labor camps. This occurred BECAUSE Mao sought to eliminate the "Four Olds" (old ideas, old culture, old customs, old habits) and restructure knowledge production around revolutionary ideology rather than traditional scholarship. This matters BECAUSE it created a ten-year gap in normal academic reproduction - no doctoral students were trained, no scholars entered the profession through conventional pathways, and classical texts were denounced as feudal remnants. As a result, when universities reopened in the late 1970s, the field faced both a lost generation and a cohort of "sent-down youth" (知识青年) who returned to academia after years of rural labor, bringing very different life experiences than pre-Cultural Revolution scholars.

## Detailed Findings

### Social Mobility: From Gentry to Workers to Middle Class

**1950s: Dominance of Traditional Scholarly Families (书香门第)**

The first generation of scholars in New China were predominantly from traditional intellectual families with multi-generational scholarly lineages. This occurred BECAUSE the Republic of China (1912-1949) educational system and classical learning remained largely within elite circles, and these families possessed extensive private libraries, traditional education, and cultural capital accumulated over generations. For example, scholars like Qian Zhongshu (钱钟书, 1910-1998) came from Wuxi's prestigious Qian family with centuries of scholarly tradition, while Chen Yinke (陈寅恪, 1890-1969) descended from a prominent Hunan family with deep connections to classical learning. This family background mattered BECAUSE it provided immersive cultural socialization - children grew up hearing classical poetry recited, witnessed scholarly debates, and absorbed literary sensibilities before formal education. As a result, these scholars possessed what Pierre Bourdieu would call embodied cultural capital: an intuitive feel for classical texts that could not be taught through formal curriculum alone.

The social background of 1950s scholars created both advantages and vulnerabilities. Their deep knowledge was invaluable for building new academic institutions, but their class origin marked them as politically suspect. This occurred BECAUSE Communist ideology viewed intellectuals from "exploiting classes" as requiring ideological remolding, leading to thought reform campaigns (思想改造运动) that forced scholars to criticize their own backgrounds and adopt Marxist perspectives. This matters BECAUSE it created profound psychological pressure and forced scholars to navigate contradictory demands - maintain scholarly excellence while denouncing the very cultural traditions that made them excellent. As a result, many scholars developed a split consciousness, publicly conforming to revolutionary ideology while privately preserving traditional scholarly values, though this precarious balance collapsed during the Cultural Revolution.

**Statistics on class origin:**
- In 1952, approximately 70-80% of university faculty came from "bourgeois" or "petty bourgeois" backgrounds, including intellectuals, merchants, and landowners ([Cultural Revolution - Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_Revolution))
- Only 20-30% came from worker, peasant, or revolutionary cadre families
- By 1958, after thought reform campaigns, many intellectuals had been removed or reassigned

**1960s-1970s: The Cultural Revolution's Assault on Traditional Intellectuals**

The Cultural Revolution fundamentally disrupted patterns of scholarly reproduction BECAUSE it explicitly targeted intellectuals as a class enemy, closed universities, and promoted workers and peasants to positions of cultural authority. Universities shut down from 1966-1970, and when they reopened in 1970-1971, admission was based on class background and political reliability rather than academic merit - the "worker-peasant-soldier students" (工农兵学员) system. This matters BECAUSE it inverted traditional meritocracy: those with the "worst" class backgrounds (intellectual families) faced the harshest persecution, while those with "red" backgrounds (workers, peasants, revolutionary cadres) gained preferential access despite often lacking basic educational preparation. As a result, the scholarly profession experienced catastrophic knowledge loss - senior scholars were persecuted, middle-generation scholars were sent to labor camps, and no properly trained younger generation emerged to replace them.

The experience of persecution varied by individual circumstances but followed patterns based on class origin and political history. Scholars from pre-1949 elite families suffered the most severe treatment - public humiliation at "struggle sessions" (批斗会), physical violence, destruction of personal libraries, and exile to rural labor. This occurred BECAUSE Red Guards viewed classical Chinese literature as "feudal poison" and its scholars as representatives of the exploiting classes who needed to be thoroughly reformed through physical labor. For example, prominent scholars were forced to clean toilets, tend pigs, or work in fields for years, disrupting or ending their research careers. This matters BECAUSE the physical and psychological trauma created lasting effects - some scholars never fully recovered their scholarly productivity, many destroyed their own research notes and manuscripts to avoid political danger, and collective memory of this period continued to shape academic risk-aversion for decades. As a result, an entire generation lost their most productive scholarly years, and much unpublished research from the pre-Cultural Revolution period was permanently lost.

The "sent-down youth" (知识青年下乡) experience created a unique cohort. Between 1968-1980, approximately 17 million urban youth were sent to rural areas for "reeducation," including many who would later become scholars. This occurred BECAUSE Mao's policy aimed to eliminate urban-rural distinctions and give educated youth "correct" class consciousness through peasant labor. This matters BECAUSE those who later entered academia (after university reopening in the late 1970s) brought very different life experiences than traditional scholars - they had experienced physical hardship, rural poverty, and political absurdity during their formative years.
